Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Understanding the Genitive Construction in Scholarly Titles
Commentator explains grammatical types of genitive in the title.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explanatory (bayaniyya) and possessive (lamia) genitive constructions in scholarly titles and explain their implications based on definitions of key terms.

Core Idea
A single genitive can change meaning depending on how you define the terms.

In this lesson, we examine how the commentator analyzes the phrase 'من تقرير عقايد الاسلام' from the book's title. The type of genitive construction depends on what 'Islam' means—whether it is identical to the beliefs, or includes actions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for interpreting scholarly titles correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genitive phrase
Locate the phrase 'عقايد الاسلام' in the title. Note that it is a genitive construction (idafa) consisting of two nouns: 'aqa'id (creeds) and al-Islam.
The entire title is 'تقريب المرام من تقرير عقايد الاسلام'.
2
Recognize the two possible definitions of 'Islam'
The commentator lists three definitions: (1) Islam equals the beliefs themselves, (2) Islam includes verbal confession, inner assent, and actions, (3) Islam is mere verbal confession. Focus on the first two for the genitive analysis.
3
Determine the type of idafa for each definition
If Islam is identical to beliefs, the genitive is explanatory (bayaniyya): 'aqa'id that are Islam. If Islam includes actions, the genitive is possessive (lamia): 'aqa'id belonging to Islam.
The commentator says: 'إن كان الإسلام عبارة عن نفس الاعتقادات فالإضافة بيانية، وإن كان عبارة عن مجموع ... فالإضافة لامية'.
4
Apply the distinction to another example
Consider the title 'شرح أصول الدين'. If 'al-din' is defined as the religion (including beliefs and practices), what type of idafa is 'أصول الدين'? Use the commentator's logic.
Think: is 'usul' part of 'din' or identical to it?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وتقريب المرام بالجر عطف على التهذيب اى هذا غاية تقريب المقصود إلى الطبايع والافهام والحمل اما على طريقة المبالغة أو التقدير هذا الكلام مقرب غاية التقريب. قوله «من تقرير عقايد الاسلام» بيان للمرام والاضافة في عقايد الاسلام بيانية ان كان الاسلام عبارة عن نفس الاعتقادات و ان كان عبارة عن مجموع الاقرار باللسان والتصديق بالجنان و العمل بالاركان او كان عبارة عن مجرد الاقرار باللسان فالاضافة لامية. قوله جعلته» تبصرة» ای مبصراً ويحتمل التجوز في الاسناد و كذا قوله: «تذكرة» قوله «لدى الافهام» بالكسر :اى تفهيم الغير اياه او تفهيمه للغير والاول للمتعلم والثاني للمعلم. قوله «من ذوى الافهام» بفتح الهمزة جمع الفهم والظرف اما في موضع الحال من فاعل يتذكر او متعلق : « يتذكر » بتضمين معنى الاخذ او التعلم اى يتذكر آخذاً او متعلماً من ذوى الافهام، وهذا ايضا يحتمل الوجهين قوله «سیما» الستی بمعنى المثل، يقال: «هماسیان» ای مثلان واصل «سیما»، «لاسیما» حذفت «لا» في اللفظ لكنه مراد و «ما» زايدة او موصولة او موصوفة، هذا اصله، ثم استعمل بمعنى خصوصاً وفيها بعده ثلاثة أوجه قوله « الحفى»: الشفيق. قوله «الحرى»: اللائق.
His saying: 'And making the desired goal approachable (Taqrib al-Maram)' is in the genitive, conjoined to 'al-Tahdhib', i.e., this is the utmost of making the intended goal approachable to natures and understandings. The interpretation is either by way of hyperbole or by estimation: 'This discourse is an approachable statement to the utmost limit.' His saying: 'from the establishment of the creeds of Islam (taqrir aqa'id al-Islam)' is an explanation of 'al-Maram', and the genitive construction in 'aqa'id al-Islam' is explanatory if Islam means the same as the beliefs themselves; if Islam means the sum of verbal confession, inner assent, and actions of the pillars, or merely verbal confession, then the genitive is possessive. His saying: 'I made it' 'a seeing (tabsira)' is 'a means of sight'; and it is possible that there is a figurative usage in the attribution, and likewise his saying: 'a reminder (tadhkira)'. His saying: 'for understandings (lada al-afham)' with kasra on the alif: i.e., making others understand it, or his understanding for others; the first is for the learner, the second for the teacher. His saying: 'from those possessing understanding (min dhawi al-afham)' with fatha on the hamza, plural of fahm. The adverb is either in the position of a circumstantial accusative from the subject of 'yatadhakkar' or is attached to 'yatadhakkar' with the inclusion of the meaning of 'taking' or 'learning', i.e., 'he remembers, taking or learning from those of understanding.' This also has two aspects. His saying: 'especially (siyya)': 'al-sitti' means the likeness; it is said: 'huma siyyan' i.e., two likes. The original of 'siyya' is 'la siyya'; the 'la' is deleted in pronunciation but intended, and 'ma' is either extra, relative pronoun, or described noun. This is its origin; then it came to be used in the sense of 'especially', and after it there are three aspects. His saying: 'al-hafiy': the compassionate. His saying: 'al-hara': the worthy.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (hashiya) on the opening line of a logical text. The commentator first explains the grammatical context of the phrase 'و تقريب المرام' stating it is conjoined to the earlier word 'التهذيب' (likely the title of the base text). He interprets that making the goal (المرام) approachable means facilitating it for natural dispositions and minds, either hyperbolically or by implying that the discourse itself is approachable. Next, he analyzes 'من تقرير عقايد الاسلام': if 'الإسلام' is identical with the beliefs (العقائد), then the genitive is explicative (بيانية); if Islam includes confession, assent, and actions, or just confession, then it is possessive (لامية). Then, he comments on 'جعلته تبصرة' and 'تذكرة', explaining that 'تبصرة' is a means of seeing, and there may be a figurative attribution. He clarifies 'لدى الافهام' as either making others understand (teacher's role) or understanding from others (learner's role). The phrase 'من ذوي الافهام' is analyzed syntactically: the adverb could be a circumstantial accusative or attached to the verb 'يتذكر'. Finally, he explains the word 'سيما' tracing it to 'لاسیما' with deletion of 'لا', and gives its meaning as 'especially'. He briefly defines 'الحفى' as compassionate and 'الحرى' as worthy. This commentary is primarily lexical and grammatical, clarifying the author's intent in his choice of words.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does the genitive in 'عقايد الاسلام' indicate if Islam is defined as the beliefs themselves?
A) Possessive (لامية)
B) Explanatory (بيانية)
C) Partitive (تبعيضية)
D) Casually (ظرفية)

Lexical Nuances of Key Terms in Islamic Introductions
Commentator glosses lexical items in the dedication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the lexical meanings and grammatical functions of the terms 'tabsira', 'tadhkira', 'siyya', 'hafiy', and 'hara' as used in scholarly introductions.

Core Idea
Authors choose words like 'tabsira' to subtly indicate the purpose of the book.

The commentator defines several words from the opening dedication: 'تبصرة' (a means of sight), 'تذكرة' (a reminder), 'سيما' (especially), 'الحفى' (compassionate), 'الحرى' (worthy). These terms are not just decorative; they convey the author's intent and the book's function. Understanding these nuances deepens appreciation of classical scholarly works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms in the passage
Locate 'تبصرة', 'تذكرة', 'سيما', 'الحفى', 'الحرى' in the Arabic text. Note that they appear in the dedication and are glossed by the commentator.
2
Understand the root meanings
For 'تبصرة', it comes from 'بصر' (to see) and means a means of sight. 'تذكرة' from 'ذكر' (to remember) means a reminder. 'سيما' originally 'لاسیما' meaning likeness, then idiomatically 'especially'. 'الحفى' means compassionate, 'الحرى' means worthy.
The commentator gives these definitions explicitly.
3
Note figurative or idiomatic usage
The commentator notes that 'تبصرة' might be a figurative attribution (تجوز في الإسناد), meaning the book itself is not literally sight-giving but metaphorically. For 'سيما', the commentator explains its historical development from a literal 'likeness' to the idiomatic 'especially'.
4
Apply to understanding authorial intent
The author describes the book as 'تبصرة' (a means of sight) and 'تذكرة' (a reminder). This suggests the book aims to provide insight and remind the reader of important truths. Similarly, the dedication to 'الحفى الحرى' (the compassionate, worthy person) indicates respect and purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وتقريب المرام بالجر عطف على التهذيب اى هذا غاية تقريب المقصود إلى الطبايع والافهام والحمل اما على طريقة المبالغة أو التقدير هذا الكلام مقرب غاية التقريب. قوله «من تقرير عقايد الاسلام» بيان للمرام والاضافة في عقايد الاسلام بيانية ان كان الاسلام عبارة عن نفس الاعتقادات و ان كان عبارة عن مجموع الاقرار باللسان والتصديق بالجنان و العمل بالاركان او كان عبارة عن مجرد الاقرار باللسان فالاضافة لامية. قوله جعلته» تبصرة» ای مبصراً ويحتمل التجوز في الاسناد و كذا قوله: «تذكرة» قوله «لدى الافهام» بالكسر :اى تفهيم الغير اياه او تفهيمه للغير والاول للمتعلم والثاني للمعلم. قوله «من ذوى الافهام» بفتح الهمزة جمع الفهم والظرف اما في موضع الحال من فاعل يتذكر او متعلق : « يتذكر » بتضمين معنى الاخذ او التعلم اى يتذكر آخذاً او متعلماً من ذوى الافهام، وهذا ايضا يحتمل الوجهين قوله «سیما» الستی بمعنى المثل، يقال: «هماسیان» ای مثلان واصل «سیما»، «لاسیما» حذفت «لا» في اللفظ لكنه مراد و «ما» زايدة او موصولة او موصوفة، هذا اصله، ثم استعمل بمعنى خصوصاً وفيها بعده ثلاثة أوجه قوله « الحفى»: الشفيق. قوله «الحرى»: اللائق.
His saying: 'And making the desired goal approachable (Taqrib al-Maram)' is in the genitive, conjoined to 'al-Tahdhib', i.e., this is the utmost of making the intended goal approachable to natures and understandings. The interpretation is either by way of hyperbole or by estimation: 'This discourse is an approachable statement to the utmost limit.' His saying: 'from the establishment of the creeds of Islam (taqrir aqa'id al-Islam)' is an explanation of 'al-Maram', and the genitive construction in 'aqa'id al-Islam' is explanatory if Islam means the same as the beliefs themselves; if Islam means the sum of verbal confession, inner assent, and actions of the pillars, or merely verbal confession, then the genitive is possessive. His saying: 'I made it' 'a seeing (tabsira)' is 'a means of sight'; and it is possible that there is a figurative usage in the attribution, and likewise his saying: 'a reminder (tadhkira)'. His saying: 'for understandings (lada al-afham)' with kasra on the alif: i.e., making others understand it, or his understanding for others; the first is for the learner, the second for the teacher. His saying: 'from those possessing understanding (min dhawi al-afham)' with fatha on the hamza, plural of fahm. The adverb is either in the position of a circumstantial accusative from the subject of 'yatadhakkar' or is attached to 'yatadhakkar' with the inclusion of the meaning of 'taking' or 'learning', i.e., 'he remembers, taking or learning from those of understanding.' This also has two aspects. His saying: 'especially (siyya)': 'al-sitti' means the likeness; it is said: 'huma siyyan' i.e., two likes. The original of 'siyya' is 'la siyya'; the 'la' is deleted in pronunciation but intended, and 'ma' is either extra, relative pronoun, or described noun. This is its origin; then it came to be used in the sense of 'especially', and after it there are three aspects. His saying: 'al-hafiy': the compassionate. His saying: 'al-hara': the worthy.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (hashiya) on the opening line of a logical text. The commentator first explains the grammatical context of the phrase 'و تقريب المرام' stating it is conjoined to the earlier word 'التهذيب' (likely the title of the base text). He interprets that making the goal (المرام) approachable means facilitating it for natural dispositions and minds, either hyperbolically or by implying that the discourse itself is approachable. Next, he analyzes 'من تقرير عقايد الاسلام': if 'الإسلام' is identical with the beliefs (العقائد), then the genitive is explicative (بيانية); if Islam includes confession, assent, and actions, or just confession, then it is possessive (لامية). Then, he comments on 'جعلته تبصرة' and 'تذكرة', explaining that 'تبصرة' is a means of seeing, and there may be a figurative attribution. He clarifies 'لدى الافهام' as either making others understand (teacher's role) or understanding from others (learner's role). The phrase 'من ذوي الافهام' is analyzed syntactically: the adverb could be a circumstantial accusative or attached to the verb 'يتذكر'. Finally, he explains the word 'سيما' tracing it to 'لاسیما' with deletion of 'لا', and gives its meaning as 'especially'. He briefly defines 'الحفى' as compassionate and 'الحرى' as worthy. This commentary is primarily lexical and grammatical, clarifying the author's intent in his choice of words.

Unpacking the Commentary: From Words to 35 Possibilities
Commentary on the author's introductory phrases and the 35 interpretations of 'first section' and 'logic'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a commentator expands on a text by providing multiple interpretations of key terms, and calculate the combinatorial possibilities from given lists.

Core Idea
One matn phrase can generate dozens of valid interpretations when you analyze its component terms.

The passage you are about to study comes from a commentary on a logic text. The commentator, Yazdi, takes the author's opening words and explains each one, showing why certain grammatical choices were made. Then he goes further: by considering seven possible meanings for 'first section' and five for 'logic', he generates thirty-five possible interpretations. This is a classic example of how commentators in the Islamic tradition 'expand' a terse text to reveal its richness. In this lesson, you will learn to appreciate this method and apply it to similar texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the glossed terms
List the words that the commentator explains: qawam, ta'yid, isam, tawakkul, i'tisam. Notice that each is given a brief definition.
2
Understand the grammatical justification
The commentator explains why the author used the definite article on 'al-qism al-awwal' (implicit well-knownness) and why 'muqaddima' is indefinite. Grasp the distinction.
3
Examine the question and answer
The commentator poses a hypothetical objection: if 'first section' means logical issues, why is the locative 'fi al-mantiq' used? He answers by reinterpreting 'first section' as words and 'logic' as meanings.
4
List the seven meanings of 'first section'
Words, meanings, written forms, composites of two, composites of three. Understand each.
These are the possible denotations of 'al-qism al-awwal'.
5
List the five meanings of 'logic'
Faculty, knowledge of all issues, sufficient amount, all issues, sufficient amount itself.
These are the possible denotations of 'al-mantiq'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «قوام» ای ما یقوم به امره (١٢٤) قوله «التأييد»: اى التقوية، من (الايد» بمعنى القوة. قوله «عصام» ای ما يحفظ به امره من الزلل (۱۲۵) قوله « وعلى الله » : قدم الظرف هيهنا لقصد الحصر وفي قوله: «به» لرعاية السجع ايضاً (١٢٦) قوله «التوكل»: هو التمسك بالحق (۱۲۷) والانقطاع عن الخلق (۱۲۸) قوله « الاعتصام هو التشبث والتمسك قوله «القسم الاول : (لما (۱۲۹) علم ضمناً (۱۳۰) من قوله في تحرير المنطق و الكلام، ان كتابه على قسمين لم يحتج الى التصريح بهذا (۱۳۱) فصح تعريف القسم الأول بلام العهد لكونه معهوداً ضمناً و هذا بخلاف المقدمة فانها لم يعلم وجودها سابقاً (۱۳۲) فلم تكن معهودة فلهذا نكرها وقال: «مقدمة». قوله «فى المنطق» ان قيل ليس المراد بالقسم الاول الا المسائل المنطقية (۱۳۳) فما توجيه الظرفية؟ (١٣٤) قلت: يجوز ان يراد بالقسم الاول (۱۳۵) الالفاظ و العبارات و بالمنطق المعاني فيكون المعنى : ان هذه الالفاظ في بيان هذه المعانى ويحتمل وجوهاً أخر. والتفصيل : ان القسم الاول عبارة عن احد معان سبعة : اما الالفاظ او المعاني او النقوش او المركب من الاثنين (١٣٦) او الثلاثة، والمنطق عبارة عن احد معان خمسة اما الملكة (۱۳۷) او العلم بجميع المسائل او بالقدر المعتد به الذي يحصل به العصمة او نفس المسائل جميعاً (۱۳۸) او نفس القدر المعتد به، فيحصل من ملاحظة الخمسة (۱۳۹) مع السبعة خمسة وثلاثون احتمالا يقدر في بعضها البيان و في بعضها التحصيل او الحصول حيث ما وجده العقل السليم مناسباً ( ١٤٠)
His saying 'qawam': that by which his affair is established (124). His saying 'al-ta'yid': strengthening, from 'al-ayd' meaning strength. His saying 'isam': that which preserves his affair from slipping (125). His saying 'and upon Allah': he advanced the adverb here for the purpose of restriction, and in his saying 'by Him' for the sake of rhyme as well (126). His saying 'al-tawakkul': it is clinging to the truth (127) and cutting off from creation (128). His saying 'al-i'tisam': it is clinging and holding fast. His saying 'the first section': since (129) it was implicitly known (130) from his saying in 'Tahrir al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam' that his book is on two sections, he did not need to explicitly mention this (131); thus it is valid to define the first section with the definite article of covenant because it is implicitly well-known. And this is unlike the introduction, for its existence was not known previously (132), so it was not well-known; therefore he made it indefinite and said 'an introduction'. His saying 'in logic': if it is said that what is intended by the first section is only the logical issues (133), so what is the justification for the locative? (134) I say: it is permissible that what is intended by the first section (135) is the words and expressions, and by logic the meanings; so the meaning is: these words are in explanation of these meanings. And other possibilities are also plausible. The detail: the first section is one of seven meanings: either words, or meanings, or written forms, or a composite of the two (136), or the three; and logic is one of five meanings: either the faculty (137), or the knowledge of all issues, or of the sufficient amount by which infallibility is obtained, or the issues themselves all (138), or the sufficient amount itself. So from considering the five (139) with the seven, there arise thirty-five possibilities; some of them yield explanation, and some yielding acquisition or occurrence, as the sound intellect finds appropriate (140).
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening lines of the book. The author (Yazdi) explains the matn's terms sequentially. First, 'qawam' is defined as that by which something stands firm. Then 'ta'yid' is strengthening from 'ayd' (power). 'Isam' is what preserves from error. The placement of 'wa 'ala Allah' is for restriction, and the repetition of 'bi-hi' is for rhyme. 'Tawakkul' is reliance on Allah and detachment from creation. 'I'tisam' is holding fast. Next, the commentator discusses why 'al-qism al-awwal' is definite: because it was implicitly known from the author's mention of two sections in 'Tahrir'. The introduction is indefinite because it was not previously known. A question arises: if the first section means logical problems, why is 'fi al-mantiq' locative? The answer provides seven possible meanings for 'al-qism al-awwal' (words, meanings, written forms, their combinations) and five for 'al-mantiq' (faculty, knowledge of all issues, sufficient amount, etc.). Multiplying gives 35 possibilities, each yielding explanation or acquisition as sound reason deems appropriate. This shows the precision of the commentary in unpacking every potential interpretation.

The Purpose of an Introduction (Muqaddima) in Logic Study
Commentary defining the introduction and its three purposes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three logical purposes of a muqaddima and explain its role in preparing the reader for a logic textbook.

Core Idea
An introduction in logic defines the science, proves its necessity, and delimits its subject.

This passage from the hashya explains what the word 'muqaddima' means in the context of a logic textbook. The commentator clarifies that the introduction serves three essential functions: defining logic, showing why it is needed, and stating its subject matter. Understanding these functions helps you appreciate why every Islamic logic text begins in a particular way.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three matters
Find in the Arabic the phrase listing three matters: رسم المنطق، بيان الحاجة إليه، موضوعه. These are the definition of logic, the statement of need, and the subject matter.
2
Distinguish the two senses of 'book'
The commentator says an introduction is a group of speech if the book is words; a group of meanings if the book is meanings. Note how the introduction functions in each case.
3
Connect to the reader's preparation
The introduction provides بصيرة (insight) and prevents misinterpretations. It prepares the mind for what follows.
4
Note the commentator's conclusion
Scholars have not added beyond words and meanings in this regard. This implies the described functions are sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «مقدمة»: اى هذه مقدمة يتبين فيها امور ثلاثة رسم المنطق و بيان الحاجة اليه و موضوعه وهى مأخوذة الجيش و المراد منها هيهنا ان كان الكتاب عبارة عن الالفاظ و العبارات طائفة من الكلام قدمت امام المقصود لارتباط المقصود بها و نفعها فيه وان كان عبارة عن المعانى فالمراد من المقدمة طائفة من المعاني يوجب الاطلاع عليها بصيرة فى الشروع وتجويز الاحتمالات الاخر في الكتاب يستدعى جوازها فى المقدمة التي هي جزئه لكن القوم لم یزیدوا على الالفاظ والمعانى فى هذا الباب شيئاً.
His saying 'Muqaddima': i.e., this is a muqaddima in which three matters are clarified: the definition of logic, the statement of the need for it, and its subject matter. It is taken from al-Jaysh. The intended meaning here is: if the book consists of words and expressions, then a group of speech placed before the intended purpose due to the connection of the intended purpose to it and its benefit therein; and if it consists of meanings, then the intended meaning of muqaddima is a group of meanings whose acquaintance necessitates insight in the commencement and allows other possibilities in the book. It requires their permissibility in the muqaddima which is its part. But the people did not add beyond words and meanings in this regard anything.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage explains the term 'مقدمة' (introduction) as used in the context of the logic text. The commentator clarifies that this introduction serves three specific purposes: defining what logic is, demonstrating why it is needed, and stating its subject matter. He notes that the introduction is derived from a source referred to as 'الجيش' (possibly a book or author). The commentator then elaborates on the dual nature of a book: if the book is understood as a collection of words and expressions, then the introduction is a piece of speech placed before the main content to facilitate understanding and benefit. If the book is considered in terms of meanings, then the introduction is a set of meanings that prepare the reader's mind and help avoid misconceptions. The commentator concludes by noting that scholars have not added anything beyond words and meanings in this context.

The First Purpose: Concepts in Logic
Author introduces the first major division of logic: the study of concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main divisions of logic (tasawwurat and tasdiqat) and state the subject of the first division.

Core Idea
Logic is divided into concepts and judgments; mastering concepts is the foundation for all later reasoning.

Classical works of logic begin by announcing the first major division: the study of concepts (tasawwurat). This sets the stage for everything that follows. In this lesson, we will parse the heading and understand how it organises the entire discipline.

Guided Path
1
Locate the heading
Find the phrase 'المقصد الأول في التصورات' in the text. This is the section heading.
The word 'مارى' after it is likely a scribal error or marginal note; ignore it for now.
2
Identify the key terms
'المقصد الأول' means 'the first purpose' and 'التصورات' means 'concepts'. Recognise that this is the first of two main divisions.
The second division is 'التصديقات' (assents).
3
Understand the division of logic
Logic is traditionally divided into two parts: conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). This heading marks the start of the first part.
This division is found in standard texts like al-Īsāghūjī and al-Shamsiyyah.
4
Recognise the importance
Every definition and argument depends on clear concepts. Without mastering tasawwur, later syllogistic reasoning becomes unsound.
In Islamic sciences, a flawed concept leads to flawed rulings and beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الاول فى التصورات مارى[؟]
The first purpose concerning concepts ... [unclear].
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a section heading. In classical works of logic, the subject is divided into two main parts: the study of concepts (tasawwurat) and the study of judgments (tasdiqat). Here, the author indicates the first purpose (al-maqsad al-awwal) is about concepts. This is a standard division, as seen in many foundational texts like al-Isaghuji and al-Shamsiyyah. The term 'مارى' is unclear; it may be a scribal error or a marginal note. The student should recognize that this heading marks the beginning of the detailed discussion of the five universals, definitions, and other conceptual tools.

Key Terms

التصورات

Key Takeaway
Logic opens with the study of concepts because every sound judgment depends on clearly defined terms.

Why Words Are Studied in Logic
Author explains why word studies are included in a logic text primarily concerned with meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why discussions of words are included in a logic book and distinguish the logician's primary concern (meanings) from secondary word studies.

Core Idea
Words serve meanings; logic is about concepts and proofs, not language.

This passage opens the first major section of the text, on concepts. The commentator addresses a natural question: why does a science of meanings spend time on words? The answer reveals the pedagogical structure of Islamic logic and the practical purpose of defining technical terms before diving into argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core concerns of logic
The passage states that the logician's primary concern is with the definer (al-mu'arraf) and the proof (al-hujja). Both are meanings, not words. Recognize that logic aims to produce knowledge: conceptual knowledge through definitions and assentive knowledge through proofs.
2
Understand why word studies are included anyway
The commentator says it is customary (ta'āruf) to include word discussions after the introduction. The reason is pedagogical: to explain the technical terms (alfāẓ muṣṭalaḥa) that will be used in the science, such as 'simple', 'compound', 'universal', etc.
This is not a digression; it is a necessary preparatory step.
3
Note the order: concepts precede assents
The passage explains that concepts are discussed first because conceptualization is either a condition for assent or a part of it. Both are prior to the conditioned. This justifies the overall structure of the book.
This reflects the epistemological priority of concepts over judgments.
4
Apply to the phrase 'indication of a word'
The matn segment 'dalālat al-lafẓ 'alā tamām mā wuḍi'a lahu' is quoted. The commentator assumes you already know that the logician studies indication but emphasizes that the focus is on meaning, not the word itself.
This sets the stage for the detailed treatment of word indication in the coming pages.

المقصد الأول في التصورات. دلالة اللفظ على تمام ما وضع له. قوله «دلالة اللفظ» قد علمت أن نظر المنطقي بالذات إنما هو في المعرف والحجة وهما من قبيل المعاني لا الألفاظ إلا أنه كما تعارف ذكر الحد والغاية والموضوع في صدر كتب المنطق ليفيد بصيرة في الشروع كذلك تعارف إيراد مباحث الألفاظ بعد المقدمة ليعين على الإفادة والاستفادة وذلك بأن يبين معاني الألفاظ المصطلحة المستعملة في محاورات أهل هذا العلم من المفرد والمركب والكلي والجزئي والمتواطئ والمشكك وغيرها، فالبحث عن الألفاظ من حيث الإفادة. وفي بعض النسخ المقصد الأول في التصورات ومعناه موضع القصد واللام فيه إشارة إلى ما علم ضمناً في قوله المعلوم التصوري والتصديق من أن كتابه مشتمل على مقصدين مقصد في التصورات ومقصد في التصديقات. وإنما قدم مباحث التصورات على التصديقات، لأن التصور كما عرفت سابقاً إما شرط للتصديق أو شطر وهم مقدمان على المشروط. (عبد الرحيم)
The first section is on concepts. The indication of a word to the entirety of what it was coined for. His saying 'the indication of a word': You have known that the logician's primary concern is with the definer and the proof, and both are of the category of meanings, not words. However, just as it is customary to mention the definition, the goal, and the subject at the beginning of logic books to provide insight at the start, likewise it is customary to introduce discussions of words after the introduction to help in giving and receiving benefit. That is by explaining the meanings of technical terms used in the discussions of the people of this science: the simple and the compound, the universal and the particular, the univocal and the equivocal, and others. So the investigation of words is from the perspective of benefit. In some copies: 'The first section is on concepts.' Its meaning is the place of intention; the definite article therein refers to what was implicitly known in his saying 'conceptual and assentive knowledge' – that his book consists of two sections: a section on concepts and a section on assents. He presented the discussions of concepts before assents only because conceptualization, as you have previously known, is either a condition for assent or a part, and both are prior to the conditioned.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is from the Yazdi Hashiya, a gloss on a logical text. It begins by naming the first section: 'The first section is on concepts.' Then it quotes a line of the original matn: 'The indication of a word to the entirety of what it was coined for.' The commentator then explains this line. He reminds the reader that the logician's primary concern is with the definer (al-mu'arraf) and the proof (al-hujja), which are meanings, not words. Nevertheless, it is customary to include topics about words after the introduction to facilitate teaching and learning. The commentator gives the rationale: to explain the meanings of technical terms used in this science, such as 'simple' and 'compound', 'universal' and 'particular', 'univocal' and 'equivocal', etc. Thus the discussion of words is for the sake of benefit. Then the commentator notes a variant reading of the section title and explains the meaning of 'al-maqsad al-awwal': it is the place of intention, and the 'lām' in 'al-maqsad' refers to what was already known from the author's earlier statement that the book has two sections: one on concepts and one on assents. He states that the section on concepts is placed first because conceptualization is either a condition for assent or a component of it, and the condition or component precedes the conditioned. This is a standard justification in logic texts.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
The Three Pillars of Any Science: Subject, Problems, and Principles
Definition of the three components of a science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define the three components (subject, problems, principles) of any Islamic science and classify given items into these categories.

Core Idea
Every science has a subject, problems, and principles—knowing this structure unlocks mastery of any Islamic discipline.

In this opening part of the passage from Yazdi's Hashiya, the author lays out the standard tripartite division of the sciences. This division is not just theoretical; it is the backbone of how Muslim scholars organized knowledge in fields like grammar, law, and theology. We will learn what each part means and why they are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
Locate the Arabic terms for the three parts: al-mawḍū‘ (subject), al-masā’il (problems), and al-mabādi’ (principles). These are stated at the beginning: 'أجزاء العلوم ثلاثة: الموضوع، المسائل، المبادي'.
The passage uses 'mabādi’' for principles, divided into conceptual and assent types.
2
Define the subject
Extract the definition: 'what is researched concerning its proper characteristics and the desired effects, meaning the entire science returns to it'. This is the entity whose essential accidents are studied.
The subject is the core focus of the science.
3
Define the problems
Find the description: 'the propositions in which this research occurs'. These are the claims investigated, usually theoretical but sometimes evident.
Problems are what the science aims to prove or discuss.
4
Distinguish the two types of principles
Principles are either conceptual (providing definitions of terms) or assent (providing premises for proofs). The passage states: 'الأولى هي المبادي التصورية والثانية هي المبادي التصديقية'.
Conceptual principles define the terms; assent principles give accepted premises.

Source Text

خاتمة: أجزاء العلوم ثلاثة: [؟] 
قوله « أجزاء العلوم» كل علم من العلوم المدونة لابد فيه من أمور:
أحدها ما يبحث فيه عن خصايصه والآثار المطلوبة منه أي يرجع جميع العلم إليه وهو الموضوع، وتلك الآثار هي الأعراض الذاتية.
الثاني: القضايا التي يقع فيها هذا البحث وهي المسائل، وهي تكون نظرية في الأغلب وقد تكون بديهية محتاجة إلى بينة كما صرحوا به وقوله تطلب في العلم يعم القبيلتين.
وأما ما وجد في بعض النسخ من التخصيص بقوله: بالبرهان فمن زيادة الناسخ على أنه يمكن توجيهه بأنه بناء على الغالب أو بأن المراد بالبرهان ما يشمل التنبيه فتنبه.
الثالث: ما يبنى عليه المسائل مما يفيد تصورات أطرافها أو التصديقات بالقضايا المأخوذة في دلائلها، فالأولى هي المبادي التصورية والثانية هي المبادي التصديقية.
قوله « الموضوعات»: هيهنا إشكال مشهور وهو أن من عد الموضوع من أجزاء العلوم إما أن يريد به نفس الموضوع أو تعريفه أو التصديق بوجوده أو بموضوعيته.
والأول مندرج في موضوعات المسائل التي هي أجزاء المسائل فلا يكون جزءاً عليحدة.
والثاني من المبادي التصورية والثالث من المبادي التصديقية فلا يكونان جزءاً عليحدة أيضاً.
والرابع من مقدمات الشروع فلا يكون جزءاً.
ويمكن الجواب: باختيار كل من الشقوق الأربعة، أما على الأول فيقال: أن نفس الموضوع وإن اندرج في المسائل لكن لشدة الاعتناء به من حيث أن المقصود من العلم معرفة أحواله والبحث عنها، عد جزءاً عليحدة، أو يقال أن المسائل ليست هي مجموع الموضوعات والمحمولات والنسب بل المحمولات المنسوبة إلى الموضوعات.
(ه) أعم من الموضوع الكلي كالكلمة والكلام في علم النحو ومن جزئياته كالفاعل والمفعول وجملة الشرط والجزاء وجملة الصلة والجزء وغيرها من أنواعه، والأجزاء إذا كانت للموضوعات أجزاء كجزئي الكلام من المسند والمسند إليه وأجزائه الجملة الشرطية وغير ذلك مما يشتمل عليه علم النحو، والمراد بالأعراض الأمور اللاحقة لها من الرفع والنصب والجر والإعراب والبناء، فلابد في النحو مثلاً تعريف الكلمة بأنه لفظ موضوع وتعريف جزئياته التي هي موضوعات لبعض المسائل بأن: «الفاعل ما أسند إليه الفعل قدم عليه وجوباً». وتعريف أداة الشرط بأنه ما دل على تعليق الثاني بوجود الأول وتعريف الإعراب مثلاً بأنه أثر يجلبه العامل في آخر الكلمة وغير ذلك. والمراد من المقدمات البينة أو المأخوذة، الاستدلالات التي ثبت بها المطلوب كالاستدلال بجواز الإضمار قبل الذكر بقول بعض الشعراء وبعدم جوازه بأن ما ورد مما يوهمه قابل للتأويل أو مجهول القائل مثلاً. والمراد من المسائل مثلاً قولهم كل فاعل مرفوع فتعريف الفاعل من المبادي التصورية التي هي حدود الموضوعات وتعريف المرفوعية من المبادي التصورية التي هي حدود الأعراض وذات الفاعل مثلاً من أجزاء المسائل والمسألة عبارة عن إثبات الرفع للفاعل فتأمل.
ومن جعل الموضوع في قوله « وحدود الموضوعات»، عبارة عن الموضوع الكلي فقط وجعل جزئياته وأنواعه داخلة في قوله: «وأعراضها»، فظني أنه سهو منه لأن النوع غير العرض وذلك واضح أيضاً من التأمل في جعل المصنف النوع مقابلاً للعرض كما لا يخفى على الفطن العارف، وأفسد من هذا القول بأن تعريف الجزئيات ليس من المبادي فليتأمل جداً فانه من مزال الأقدام (میرزا رضا ره).
Conclusion: The parts of the sciences are three: [?]. He said 'The parts of the sciences': Every science from the compiled sciences necessarily includes matters: One of them is that which is researched concerning its proper characteristics and the desired effects, meaning the entire science returns to it, and it is the subject, and those effects are the essential accidents. Second: The propositions in which this research occurs, and they are the problems, and they are mostly theoretical and may be evident needing proof, as they stated, and his statement 'it is sought in the science' covers both types. As for what is found in some copies of the specification by his saying 'by demonstration', it is from the copyist's addition, and it is possible to justify it by saying it is based on the majority or that by 'demonstration' he means what includes alerting, so be alert. Third: That upon which the problems are built, which provides the conceptualizations of their parts or the assents to the propositions taken in their proofs. The first are the conceptual principles and the second are the assent principles. He said 'the subjects': Here there is a famous objection: Whoever counts the subject as part of the sciences intends either the subject itself, or its definition, or the assent to its existence, or to its subjectivity. The first is included in the subjects of the problems which are parts of the problems, so it is not a separate part. The second is from the conceptual principles, and the third from the assent principles, so they are not separate parts either. And the fourth is from the introductory premises, so it is not a part. It is possible to answer: by choosing each of the four branches. As for the first, it is said: The subject itself, even though it is included in the problems, due to the intense concern for it, since the purpose of the science is knowledge of its states and research about it, it is counted as a separate part. Or it is said that the problems are not the totality of subjects and predicates and relations, but rather the predicates related to the subjects. (It) includes the universal subject like 'word' and 'speech' in the science of grammar, and its particulars like the agent and object, the conditional and result clause, the relative clause and the part, and other types. Parts when they are parts of the subjects, like the two parts of speech (predicate and subject) and its parts the conditional sentence, etc., which the science of grammar includes. By 'accidents' is meant the things that attach to them like raf', nasb, jarr, i'rab, and bina'. So in grammar, for example, it is necessary to define 'word' as an utterance that is posited, and to define its particulars which are subjects for some problems such as: 'The agent is that which the verb is attributed to and obligatorily precedes it'. And to define the conditional particle as that which indicates the dependency of the second on the existence of the first, and to define i'rab, for example, as an effect brought by the operator at the end of the word, etc. By 'evident or taken premises' is meant the inferences by which the desired is proven, like inferring the permissibility of pronominal anaphora before mention by a poet's saying, and its impermissibility that what appears to indicate it is either interpretable or its speaker is unknown. By 'problems' is meant, for example, their saying 'Every agent is marfu'. So defining the agent is from the conceptual principles which are the definitions of subjects, and defining marfu'iyya is from the conceptual principles which are the definitions of accidents, and the essence of the agent is from the parts of the problems, and the problem is the affirmation of raf' for the agent, so reflect. And whoever takes 'subject' in his saying 'and the definitions of the subjects' to mean only the universal subject and includes its particulars and types in his saying 'and their accidents', I think it is an oversight from him because the type is not an accident, and that is clear from reflection on the author making type opposite to accident, as is not hidden from the astute and knowledgeable. And more corrupt than this statement is that defining the particulars is not from the principles, so let one contemplate greatly, for it is a slippery slope. (Mirza Raza).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines the three parts of any science and defends the inclusion of the subject as a separate part. The author begins by stating that every organized science necessarily comprises three things: the subject (mawḍū'), which is the central entity whose properties are investigated; the problems (masā'il), which are the propositions discussed (usually theoretical, occasionally evident but requiring proof); and the principles (mabādi'), which provide the conceptualizations or assents needed for the problems. The conceptual principles give definitions of terms, while the assent principles provide premises for proofs. Then the author addresses a famous objection: if the subject is already included in the problems or principles, why count it separately? He presents four possibilities: the subject itself, its definition, the assent to its existence, or the assent to its being a subject. The first is part of the problems, the second and third are principles, the fourth is preliminary. To this, he gives an answer: either the subject is considered separate due to its central importance, or the problems are not the subjects themselves but the predicates attributed to them. The rest of the passage illustrates with examples from Arabic grammar (nahw), showing how definitions of words like 'word', 'agent', 'conditional particle' are conceptual principles, while the rulings (e.g., every agent is marfu') are the problems. The author ends by cautioning against a misinterpretation that would include types under accidents, stressing that type and accident are distinct. This passage is typical of a hashiya (supercommentary) clarifying the organization of sciences as found in logic texts.

Key Terms
أجزاء العلوم (ajzā' al-‘ulūm): the three components of a science
الموضوع (al-mawḍū‘): the subject matter of a science
المسائل (al-masā’il): the problems or propositions investigated
المبادي (al-mabādi’): the principles, either conceptual (tasawwurī) or assent (tasdīqī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Every organized Islamic science has three essential parts: the subject (its focus), the problems (its propositions), and the principles (its definitions and premises).

Introduction and Front Matter
advanced
10 min
Defending the Subject as a Separate Part of Science
Refutation of an objection concerning the subject's inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection against counting the subject as a separate part and evaluate the author's defense using logical analysis.

Core Idea
The subject is counted separately not because it is absent elsewhere but because of its central importance.

After defining the three parts, the author addresses a famous objection: if the subject itself appears in the problems, or its definition in principles, then why count it separately? This objection challenges the very structure of the sciences. The author provides a detailed response showing both the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Find the sentence: 'هيهنا إشكال مشهور وهو أن من عد الموضوع من أجزاء العلوم إما أن يريد به نفس الموضوع أو تعريفه أو التصديق بوجوده أو بموضوعيته'. The objector claims the subject is not a separate part because it falls under problems or principles.
The objection identifies four possible intentions for counting the subject.
2
Analyze the four possibilities
For each intention, explain why it seems redundant: (1) subject itself is part of problems; (2) its definition is a conceptual principle; (3) assent to its existence is an assent principle; (4) assent to its subjectivity is a preliminary premise.
This shows the logical basis of the objection.
3
Examine the author's first answer
The author says that even though the subject is included in the problems, it is counted separately 'لشدة الاعتناء به' (due to intense concern for it) because the whole science aims to know its states.
This is a pragmatic rather than a purely logical justification.
4
Examine the author's second answer
Alternatively, the problems are not the subjects themselves but the predicates attributed to them: 'المسائل ليست هي مجموع الموضوعات والمحمولات والنسب بل المحمولات المنسوبة إلى الموضوعات'. This means the subject term of a problem is not the problem itself.
This redefines what counts as a problem to exclude the subject term.

Source Text

خاتمة: أجزاء العلوم ثلاثة: [؟] 
قوله « أجزاء العلوم» كل علم من العلوم المدونة لابد فيه من أمور:
أحدها ما يبحث فيه عن خصايصه والآثار المطلوبة منه أي يرجع جميع العلم إليه وهو الموضوع، وتلك الآثار هي الأعراض الذاتية.
الثاني: القضايا التي يقع فيها هذا البحث وهي المسائل، وهي تكون نظرية في الأغلب وقد تكون بديهية محتاجة إلى بينة كما صرحوا به وقوله تطلب في العلم يعم القبيلتين.
وأما ما وجد في بعض النسخ من التخصيص بقوله: بالبرهان فمن زيادة الناسخ على أنه يمكن توجيهه بأنه بناء على الغالب أو بأن المراد بالبرهان ما يشمل التنبيه فتنبه.
الثالث: ما يبنى عليه المسائل مما يفيد تصورات أطرافها أو التصديقات بالقضايا المأخوذة في دلائلها، فالأولى هي المبادي التصورية والثانية هي المبادي التصديقية.
قوله « الموضوعات»: هيهنا إشكال مشهور وهو أن من عد الموضوع من أجزاء العلوم إما أن يريد به نفس الموضوع أو تعريفه أو التصديق بوجوده أو بموضوعيته.
والأول مندرج في موضوعات المسائل التي هي أجزاء المسائل فلا يكون جزءاً عليحدة.
والثاني من المبادي التصورية والثالث من المبادي التصديقية فلا يكونان جزءاً عليحدة أيضاً.
والرابع من مقدمات الشروع فلا يكون جزءاً.
ويمكن الجواب: باختيار كل من الشقوق الأربعة، أما على الأول فيقال: أن نفس الموضوع وإن اندرج في المسائل لكن لشدة الاعتناء به من حيث أن المقصود من العلم معرفة أحواله والبحث عنها، عد جزءاً عليحدة، أو يقال أن المسائل ليست هي مجموع الموضوعات والمحمولات والنسب بل المحمولات المنسوبة إلى الموضوعات.
(ه) أعم من الموضوع الكلي كالكلمة والكلام في علم النحو ومن جزئياته كالفاعل والمفعول وجملة الشرط والجزاء وجملة الصلة والجزء وغيرها من أنواعه، والأجزاء إذا كانت للموضوعات أجزاء كجزئي الكلام من المسند والمسند إليه وأجزائه الجملة الشرطية وغير ذلك مما يشتمل عليه علم النحو، والمراد بالأعراض الأمور اللاحقة لها من الرفع والنصب والجر والإعراب والبناء، فلابد في النحو مثلاً تعريف الكلمة بأنه لفظ موضوع وتعريف جزئياته التي هي موضوعات لبعض المسائل بأن: «الفاعل ما أسند إليه الفعل قدم عليه وجوباً». وتعريف أداة الشرط بأنه ما دل على تعليق الثاني بوجود الأول وتعريف الإعراب مثلاً بأنه أثر يجلبه العامل في آخر الكلمة وغير ذلك. والمراد من المقدمات البينة أو المأخوذة، الاستدلالات التي ثبت بها المطلوب كالاستدلال بجواز الإضمار قبل الذكر بقول بعض الشعراء وبعدم جوازه بأن ما ورد مما يوهمه قابل للتأويل أو مجهول القائل مثلاً. والمراد من المسائل مثلاً قولهم كل فاعل مرفوع فتعريف الفاعل من المبادي التصورية التي هي حدود الموضوعات وتعريف المرفوعية من المبادي التصورية التي هي حدود الأعراض وذات الفاعل مثلاً من أجزاء المسائل والمسألة عبارة عن إثبات الرفع للفاعل فتأمل.
ومن جعل الموضوع في قوله « وحدود الموضوعات»، عبارة عن الموضوع الكلي فقط وجعل جزئياته وأنواعه داخلة في قوله: «وأعراضها»، فظني أنه سهو منه لأن النوع غير العرض وذلك واضح أيضاً من التأمل في جعل المصنف النوع مقابلاً للعرض كما لا يخفى على الفطن العارف، وأفسد من هذا القول بأن تعريف الجزئيات ليس من المبادي فليتأمل جداً فانه من مزال الأقدام (میرزا رضا ره).
Conclusion: The parts of the sciences are three: [?]. He said 'The parts of the sciences': Every science from the compiled sciences necessarily includes matters: One of them is that which is researched concerning its proper characteristics and the desired effects, meaning the entire science returns to it, and it is the subject, and those effects are the essential accidents. Second: The propositions in which this research occurs, and they are the problems, and they are mostly theoretical and may be evident needing proof, as they stated, and his statement 'it is sought in the science' covers both types. As for what is found in some copies of the specification by his saying 'by demonstration', it is from the copyist's addition, and it is possible to justify it by saying it is based on the majority or that by 'demonstration' he means what includes alerting, so be alert. Third: That upon which the problems are built, which provides the conceptualizations of their parts or the assents to the propositions taken in their proofs. The first are the conceptual principles and the second are the assent principles. He said 'the subjects': Here there is a famous objection: Whoever counts the subject as part of the sciences intends either the subject itself, or its definition, or the assent to its existence, or to its subjectivity. The first is included in the subjects of the problems which are parts of the problems, so it is not a separate part. The second is from the conceptual principles, and the third from the assent principles, so they are not separate parts either. And the fourth is from the introductory premises, so it is not a part. It is possible to answer: by choosing each of the four branches. As for the first, it is said: The subject itself, even though it is included in the problems, due to the intense concern for it, since the purpose of the science is knowledge of its states and research about it, it is counted as a separate part. Or it is said that the problems are not the totality of subjects and predicates and relations, but rather the predicates related to the subjects. (It) includes the universal subject like 'word' and 'speech' in the science of grammar, and its particulars like the agent and object, the conditional and result clause, the relative clause and the part, and other types. Parts when they are parts of the subjects, like the two parts of speech (predicate and subject) and its parts the conditional sentence, etc., which the science of grammar includes. By 'accidents' is meant the things that attach to them like raf', nasb, jarr, i'rab, and bina'. So in grammar, for example, it is necessary to define 'word' as an utterance that is posited, and to define its particulars which are subjects for some problems such as: 'The agent is that which the verb is attributed to and obligatorily precedes it'. And to define the conditional particle as that which indicates the dependency of the second on the existence of the first, and to define i'rab, for example, as an effect brought by the operator at the end of the word, etc. By 'evident or taken premises' is meant the inferences by which the desired is proven, like inferring the permissibility of pronominal anaphora before mention by a poet's saying, and its impermissibility that what appears to indicate it is either interpretable or its speaker is unknown. By 'problems' is meant, for example, their saying 'Every agent is marfu'. So defining the agent is from the conceptual principles which are the definitions of subjects, and defining marfu'iyya is from the conceptual principles which are the definitions of accidents, and the essence of the agent is from the parts of the problems, and the problem is the affirmation of raf' for the agent, so reflect. And whoever takes 'subject' in his saying 'and the definitions of the subjects' to mean only the universal subject and includes its particulars and types in his saying 'and their accidents', I think it is an oversight from him because the type is not an accident, and that is clear from reflection on the author making type opposite to accident, as is not hidden from the astute and knowledgeable. And more corrupt than this statement is that defining the particulars is not from the principles, so let one contemplate greatly, for it is a slippery slope. (Mirza Raza).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines the three parts of any science and defends the inclusion of the subject as a separate part. The author begins by stating that every organized science necessarily comprises three things: the subject (mawḍū'), which is the central entity whose properties are investigated; the problems (masā'il), which are the propositions discussed (usually theoretical, occasionally evident but requiring proof); and the principles (mabādi'), which provide the conceptualizations or assents needed for the problems. The conceptual principles give definitions of terms, while the assent principles provide premises for proofs. Then the author addresses a famous objection: if the subject is already included in the problems or principles, why count it separately? He presents four possibilities: the subject itself, its definition, the assent to its existence, or the assent to its being a subject. The first is part of the problems, the second and third are principles, the fourth is preliminary. To this, he gives an answer: either the subject is considered separate due to its central importance, or the problems are not the subjects themselves but the predicates attributed to them. The rest of the passage illustrates with examples from Arabic grammar (nahw), showing how definitions of words like 'word', 'agent', 'conditional particle' are conceptual principles, while the rulings (e.g., every agent is marfu') are the problems. The author ends by cautioning against a misinterpretation that would include types under accidents, stressing that type and accident are distinct. This passage is typical of a hashiya (supercommentary) clarifying the organization of sciences as found in logic texts.

Key Terms
الإشكال (al-ishkāl): the objection
الموضوع (al-mawḍū‘): the subject
المسائل (al-masā’il): the problems
المبادي التصورية (al-mabādi’ al-tasawwuriyya): conceptual principles
المبادي التصديقية (al-mabādi’ al-tasdīqiyya): assent principles

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the four intentions considered in the objection against counting the subject as a separate part?
A) The subject itself
B) The definition of the subject
C) The assent to the existence of the subject
D) The assent to the universality of the subject

Key Takeaway
The subject's separate status is defensible either by appealing to its central importance or by redefining problems as merely the predicates attributed to subjects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the science of Tafsir. Its subject is the Quran. An objector says the Quran itself appears in the problems (e.g., 'The Quran is the word of God'), so it is not a separate part. Using the author's second answer (problems are predicates, not subjects), write a brief defense of why the subject (the Quran) should still be counted separately.
?
In the science of Hadith, what is the subject? Give one example of a problem and a conceptual principle. Then explain why the subject is not redundant with the problem.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Applying the Tripartite Division: The Example of Arabic Grammar
Illustration of the three parts using Arabic grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify items from Arabic grammar (nahw) into subject, problems, and principles using the criteria from the passage.

Core Idea
The tripartite division comes alive when applied to a real Islamic science like grammar.

The author uses Arabic grammar (nahw) to concretely illustrate the tripartite division. He shows how the universal subject, particulars, definitions, and problems map onto the components. This lesson will walk through that example so you can apply it to other sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal subject in nahw
Find the sentence: 'أعم من الموضوع الكلي كالكلمة والكلام في علم النحو'. The universal subject of nahw is 'word (kalima) and speech (kalām)'.
These are the broadest categories studied.
2
Identify the particulars of the subject
The passage lists examples: 'الفاعل والمفعول وجملة الشرط والجزاء وجملة الصلة والجزء وغيرها'. These are specific types like agent, object, conditional clause, etc.
Particulars are subdivisions of the universal subject.
3
Identify conceptual principles (definitions)
The passage gives examples: definition of 'word' (لفظ موضوع), definition of 'agent' (ما أسند إليه الفعل قدم عليه وجوباً), definition of 'conditional particle'. These are conceptual principles.
Conceptual principles define the terms used in the science.
4
Identify a problem
The passage states: 'والمراد من المسائل مثلاً قولهم كل فاعل مرفوع'. So a problem is the proposition 'Every agent is marfu''.
Problems are the claims that the science investigates.
5
Note the warning about confusing type and accident
The author criticizes those who include 'types' (like agent) under accidents. He says 'النوع غير العرض' (the type is not an accident). Types are subjects, not accidental properties.
This shows the importance of correct classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة: أجزاء العلوم ثلاثة: [؟] 
قوله « أجزاء العلوم» كل علم من العلوم المدونة لابد فيه من أمور:
أحدها ما يبحث فيه عن خصايصه والآثار المطلوبة منه أي يرجع جميع العلم إليه وهو الموضوع، وتلك الآثار هي الأعراض الذاتية.
الثاني: القضايا التي يقع فيها هذا البحث وهي المسائل، وهي تكون نظرية في الأغلب وقد تكون بديهية محتاجة إلى بينة كما صرحوا به وقوله تطلب في العلم يعم القبيلتين.
وأما ما وجد في بعض النسخ من التخصيص بقوله: بالبرهان فمن زيادة الناسخ على أنه يمكن توجيهه بأنه بناء على الغالب أو بأن المراد بالبرهان ما يشمل التنبيه فتنبه.
الثالث: ما يبنى عليه المسائل مما يفيد تصورات أطرافها أو التصديقات بالقضايا المأخوذة في دلائلها، فالأولى هي المبادي التصورية والثانية هي المبادي التصديقية.
قوله « الموضوعات»: هيهنا إشكال مشهور وهو أن من عد الموضوع من أجزاء العلوم إما أن يريد به نفس الموضوع أو تعريفه أو التصديق بوجوده أو بموضوعيته.
والأول مندرج في موضوعات المسائل التي هي أجزاء المسائل فلا يكون جزءاً عليحدة.
والثاني من المبادي التصورية والثالث من المبادي التصديقية فلا يكونان جزءاً عليحدة أيضاً.
والرابع من مقدمات الشروع فلا يكون جزءاً.

(ه) أعم من الموضوع الكلي كالكلمة والكلام في علم النحو ومن جزئياته كالفاعل والمفعول وجملة الشرط والجزاء وجملة الصلة والجزء وغيرها من أنواعه، والأجزاء إذا كانت للموضوعات أجزاء كجزئي الكلام من المسند والمسند إليه وأجزائه الجملة الشرطية وغير ذلك مما يشتمل عليه علم النحو، والمراد بالأعراض الأمور اللاحقة لها من الرفع والنصب والجر والإعراب والبناء، فلابد في النحو مثلاً تعريف الكلمة بأنه لفظ موضوع وتعريف جزئياته التي هي موضوعات لبعض المسائل بأن: «الفاعل ما أسند إليه الفعل قدم عليه وجوباً». وتعريف أداة الشرط بأنه ما دل على تعليق الثاني بوجود الأول وتعريف الإعراب مثلاً بأنه أثر يجلبه العامل في آخر الكلمة وغير ذلك. والمراد من المقدمات البينة أو المأخوذة، الاستدلالات التي ثبت بها المطلوب كالاستدلال بجواز الإضمار قبل الذكر بقول بعض الشعراء وبعدم جوازه بأن ما ورد مما يوهمه قابل للتأويل أو مجهول القائل مثلاً. والمراد من المسائل مثلاً قولهم كل فاعل مرفوع فتعريف الفاعل من المبادي التصورية التي هي حدود الموضوعات وتعريف المرفوعية من المبادي التصورية التي هي حدود الأعراض وذات الفاعل مثلاً من أجزاء المسائل والمسألة عبارة عن إثبات الرفع للفاعل فتأمل.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Purpose, Benefit, and Title of a Science
Author explains the first three of the eight heads

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the purpose (al-gharad), benefit (al-manfa'a), and title (al-simma) of a science as presented in classical Islamic introductions.

Core Idea
Purpose motivates the author; benefit attracts the student; title gives a summary.

When you open a classical Islamic text, the author often begins by stating the purpose, benefit, and title of the science. This lesson examines these three 'heads' (ru'us) that orient the reader and set the stage for the detailed exposition that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three heads
Locate al-gharad (purpose), al-manfa'a (benefit), and al-simma (title) in the Arabic passage. Notice they are listed as the first three of the eight heads.
2
Understand each head's function
The purpose ensures the investigation is not in vain; the benefit motivates the student to endure hardship; the title provides a concise overview of the science.
Distinguish purpose (for the author/compiler) from benefit (for the student).
3
Apply to the science of logic
Recall from earlier that the purpose and goal of logic is 'protection from error' (al-'isma). The benefit is that it gives a measure (mi'yār) to distinguish truth from falsehood. The title 'Logic' (al-mantiq) summarizes its subject.
This shows how each head concretely applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اجزاء العلوم القدماء يذكرون ما يسمونه الرؤس الثمانية : الاول: الغرض، لئلا يكون النظر فيه عبثاً. الثاني: المنفعة وهى ما يتشوقه الكل طبعاً لينشط الطالب ويتحمّل المشقة. الثالث: السمة و هى عنوان العلم ليكون عنده اجمال ما يفصله. الشروع في مقاصد العلم سواء كان داخلاً فى العلم فيكون من المبادى المصطلحة السابقة كتصور الموضوع والاعراض الذاتية و التصديقات التي يتألف منها قياسات العلم او خارجاً عنه يتوقف عليه الشروع على وجه الخبرة و يسمى مقدمات كمعرفة الحد و الغاية و بيان الموضوع والاستمداد، والفرق بين المقدمات والمبادى بهذا المعنى مما لا ينبغي ان يشتبه فان المقدمات خارجة عن العلم لا محالة بخلاف المبادى فتبصر.

قوله « يذكرون» اى فى صدر كتبهم على انها من المقدمات او من المبادى بالمعنى الاعم.

قوله «الغرض»: اعلم ان ما يترتب على فعل ان كان باعثاً للفاعل على صدور ذلك الفعل منه، يسمى غرضاً و علة غائية والا يسمى فايدة و منفعة و غاية، قالوا: افعال الله تعالى لا تعلل بالاغراض و ان اشتملت على غايات و منافع لا تحصى فكان مقصود المصنف ان القدماء كانوا يذكرون في صدر كتبهم ما كان سبباً حاملاً على تدوين المُدوّن الاول لهذا العلم ثم يعقبونه بما يشتمل مصلحة حتى يميل اليها عموم الطبايع ان كانت لهذا العلم منفعة ومصلحة سوى الغرض الباعث للواضع الاول وقد عرفت فى صدر الكتاب الغرض و الغاية من علم المنطق و هما العصمة فتذكر.

قوله « الثالث السمة السمة في اللغة العلامة و كان المقصود هيهنا
Regarding the parts of the sciences, the ancients used to mention what they call the eight heads: First: the purpose, so that the investigation not be in vain. Second: the benefit, which is what everyone naturally desires, so that the student becomes active and bears hardship. Third: the title, which is the heading of the science, so that he has a summary of what he details. The commencement into the aims of the science, whether it is inside the science, then it is among the aforementioned technical principles, like the conception of the subject and the essential accidents and the assents from which the syllogisms of the science are composed, or outside it, upon which the commencement depends with expertise, and it is called muqaddimat, like knowledge of the definition, the goal, the explanation of the subject, and the borrowing [from other sciences]. And the difference between muqaddimat and mabadi' in this sense should not be confused, for muqaddimat are inevitably outside the science, unlike mabadi'. So be insightful.

His saying: 'They mention' i.e., in the opening of their books, as part of the muqaddimat or of the mabadi' in the general sense.

His saying: 'The purpose': Know that what results from an action, if it is a motivator for the agent to produce that action, is called purpose and final cause, otherwise it is called benefit, utility, and goal. They said: The actions of God are not caused by purposes, even if they include countless goals and benefits. So what the author intended is that the ancients used to mention at the beginning of their books what was a cause motivating the first compiler to write this science, then they followed it with what includes a benefit, so that the general dispositions incline towards it, if this science has a benefit and advantage besides the purpose that motivated the first originator. And you have known at the beginning of the book the purpose and goal of the science of logic, and they are protection (from error). So remember.

His saying: 'Third: al-simma': Al-simma in language is the mark, and what was intended here...
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage explains the 'eight heads' (al-ru'us al-thamaniya) that ancient scholars placed at the beginning of their works to orient the reader. The author, in his hashia, expands on the first three: (1) al-gharad (purpose) — to ensure the investigation is not vain; (2) al-manfa'a (benefit) — to motivate the student by appealing to natural desire; (3) al-simma (title) — to give a concise overview of the science. The text then digresses into a distinction between mabadi' (principles) and muqaddimat (premises) in the context of commencing a science: if the material is part of the science, it is mabadi'; if external but necessary to begin, it is muqaddimat. This distinction is crucial to avoid confusion. A marginal note clarifies that 'they mention' refers to the opening of their books. Another note elaborates on purpose and benefit: purpose is the motivating cause for the agent (e.g., the first compiler), while benefit is what the student gains. It is noted that God's actions are not caused by purposes, but they contain benefits. The author reminds the reader that for logic, the purpose and goal is 'protection' (al-'isma) from error. The passage ends with a lexical note on al-simma meaning 'mark' or 'title'. Overall, this lesson provides a methodological framework for understanding how classical scholars structured their introductions.

المنفعة
السمة

A) To ensure the investigation is not in vain
B) To motivate the student to endure hardship
C) To provide a summary of the science before detailing
D) To show that God's actions have no purpose

Key Takeaway
Purpose, benefit, and title are three distinct introductory heads that orient both author and student to the science.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate

Principles vs. Premises: Mabadi' and Muqaddimat
Author distinguishes mabadi' from muqaddimat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given introductory element of a science as either a mabda' (principle) or a muqaddima (premise) based on whether it is internal or external to the science.

Core Idea
What is internal to the science is a principle; what is external but necessary is a premise.

When starting a science, scholars distinguish between material that belongs to the science itself (mabadi') and material that is needed to begin but is outside the science (muqaddimat). This lesson unpacks that distinction from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction
Find the sentence starting with 'الشروع في مقاصد العلم' in Arabic. It says that what is inside the science is mabadi' (e.g., conception of the subject, essential accidents, assents forming syllogisms), and what is outside but needed to start is muqaddimat (e.g., knowledge of the definition, goal, subject, borrowing).
2
Apply examples from logic
For logic: definitions of tasawwur/tasdiq are muqaddimat (external), while the rules of syllogism are mabadi' (internal). The passage warns not to confuse them.
3
Test your understanding
Given a statement like 'The subject of logic is tasawwur and tasdiq,' determine if it is a mabda' or muqaddima. (It is muqaddima because it is external, defining the subject.)

The Purpose of a Science: Defining al-Gharad
Author enumerates the eight heads and defines the first (al-gharad) with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-gharad (purpose) and al-manfa'a (benefit) as used in the prolegomena to Islamic sciences, and identify the purpose of logic according to this tradition.

Core Idea
The purpose of a science is its final cause; benefit is its attraction.

The ancient scholars of Islam paid careful attention to how they introduced a science. They often began with a set of preliminary discussions known as the 'eight heads' (al-ru'us al-thamaniya). The very first of these is al-gharad, the motivating purpose behind composing the science. Understanding this concept helps you see why a science was founded and what it aims to achieve—above and beyond any subsequent benefits that may attract students.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eight heads
The passage lists the first five of the eight heads: al-gharad (purpose), al-manfa'a (benefit), al-sima (name), al-mu'allif (author), and 'which science it is'. Recognize that these heads form a standard preamble for classical Islamic works.
The remaining three heads are not listed here, but the pattern signals the author's intent to follow established scholarly conventions.
2
Analyze the definition of al-gharad
The passage defines al-gharad as the result of an action that motivates the agent to perform that action. If the result is not a motivator, it is called a benefit (manfa'a) or end (ghaya). Compare these two categories carefully.
The key is the causality: gharad is a 'final cause' (illa ghā'iyya).
3
Apply the distinction to the science of logic
The passage states that the purpose (gharad) of logic is 'ismah (infallibility), i.e., preserving the mind from error in reasoning. The benefits (manafi') of logic may be many, such as acquiring correct knowledge or refuting opponents, but these are not the primary motivator for the science's foundation.
This distinction explains why logicians begin by stating the gharad first, then add benefits to encourage students.
4
Examine the theological exception
The passage notes that Allah's actions are not subject to purposes (gharad) in the sense of external motivation, though they contain countless benefits. This highlights that the concept of gharad applies paradigmatically to human authors, not to divine acts.
This nuance prevents misunderstanding when scholars say 'the purpose of the Shari'a is...' – they refer to benefits, not motivators for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اجزاء العلوم القدماء يذكرون ما يسمونه الرؤس الثمانية: الأول: الغرض، لئلا يكون النظر طلبا فيه عبثا. الثاني: المنفعة وهى ما يتشوقه الكل طبعاً لينشط الطالب ويتحمّل المشقة. الثالث: السمة و هى عنوان العلم ليكون عنده اجمال ما يفصله. الرابع: المؤلف ليسكن قلب المتعلم. الخامس: انه من اى علم هو ليطلب فيه ما يليق به. قوله « الغرض»: اعلم ان ما يترتب على فعل ان كان باعثاً للفاعل على صدور ذلك الفعل منه، يسمى غرضاً و علة غائية والا يسمى فايدة و منفعة و غاية، قالوا: افعال الله تعالى لا تعلل بالاغراض و ان اشتملت على غايات و منافع لا تحصى فكان مقصود المصنف ان القدماء كانوا يذكرون في صدر كتبهم ما كان سبباً حاملاً على تدوين المُدوّن الاول لهذا العلم ثم يعقبونه بما يشتمل مصلحة حتى يميل اليها عموم الطبايع ان كانت لهذا العلم منفعة ومصلحة سوى الغرض الباعث للواضع الاول وقد عرفت فى صدر الكتاب الغرض و الغاية من علم المنطق و هما العصمة فتذكر.
In the parts of the sciences, the ancients mention what they call the eight heads: First: the purpose, so that the investigation is not a vain seeking (but rather) effort. Second: the benefit, and it is what everyone naturally desires to motivate the student and endure the effort. Third: the name, and it is the title of the science so that he has an overview of what it details. Fourth: the author, to settle the student's heart. Fifth: which science it is, so that one seeks what befits it. His statement 'al-gharad': Know that what results from an action, if it is a motivator for the agent to perform that action, is called a purpose and final cause; otherwise it is called a benefit, benefit, and end. They said: The actions of Allah Most High are not caused by purposes, even though they include countless ends and benefits. So the intention of the author was that the ancients used to mention at the beginning of their books what was a cause driving the first compiler to compose this science, then they followed it with something containing benefit so that the general dispositions incline towards it, if there is for this science a benefit and advantage beyond the purpose that motivated the first founder. And you have known at the beginning of the book the purpose and end of the science of logic, and they are 'ismah (infallibility), so remember.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage introduces the 'eight heads' that ancient authors used to place at the beginning of their works on sciences. It then defines the first head: al-gharad (the purpose). The purpose is that which motivates the author to write; if it is the motivating cause it is called 'gharad' or 'final cause' (`illa ghā'iyya`); if it is not the motivator but rather a subsequent benefit, it is called 'fā'ida' (benefit), 'manfa'a' (benefit), or 'ghāya' (end). Importantly, the scholars assert that Allah's actions are not subject to purposes in the sense of being motivated by an external goal, even though they contain countless benefits. The author's intention in mentioning this is to explain the ancient practice: they first stated the purpose that drove the first compiler of the science, then they added the benefit to attract students. The purpose of logic is identified as 'ismah' (infallibility) – preserving the mind from error in thought. The passage thus sets the methodological groundwork for understanding why sciences are introduced with certain formal elements.

الغرض
المنفعة
السمة
العصمة

Key Takeaway
The purpose (gharad) of a science is its final cause, distinct from its benefits (manafi')—this distinction helps structure the foundational discussions of any Islamic science.

The Benefit and Name of Logic: Etymology of 'Mantiq'
Author briefly defines the second and third heads with commentary on the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the benefit of logic and how its name is derived from the two senses of nutq.

Core Idea
Logic's name reflects its dual role in perfecting both outward and inward speech.

In this passage, the author covers the second and third introductory heads: the benefit (al-manfa'a) and the name (al-sima) of logic. The benefit is what naturally attracts students and motivates them to endure the rigours of learning. The name gives an overview of the science's content. The commentary then unpacks the etymology of 'mantiq', showing how it derives from 'nutq'—which means both vocal speech and the inner perception of universals. Understanding this connection helps you grasp the scope of logic from its very name.

Guided Path
1
Identify the benefit (manfa'a) of logic
Find the sentence that defines benefit. It says it is what everyone naturally desires to motivate the student and endure hardship.
2
Understand the purpose of stating the name (sima)
The name serves as a title that gives an overview of what the science details. It is like a summary.
3
Examine the two meanings of 'nutq'
The passage distinguishes outward nutq (talking) and inward nutq (perception of universals). This is crucial for the etymology.
Inward nutq is equated with intellect ('aql).
4
See how 'mantiq' is derived from 'nutq'
'Mantiq' can be a verbal noun meaning 'nutq' (exaggeration of its role) or a noun of place (locus of nutq). Both interpretations emphasize that logic perfects speech.
5
Connect the etymology to the science's purpose
The name itself gives a summary indication (ishāra ijmāliyya) of the purposes of logic. This reinforces that logic deals with both speech and thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: المنفعة وهى ما يتشوقه الكل طبعاً لينشط الطالب ويتحمّل المشقة. الثالث: السمة و هى عنوان العلم ليكون عنده اجمال ما يفصله. قوله « الثالث السمة السمة في اللغة العلامة و كان المقصود هيهنا الإشارة الى وجه تسمية العلم كما يقال : انما المنطق منطقاً لان النطق يطلق على الظاهرى و هو التكلم وعلى الباطني و هو ادراك الكليات و هذا العلم يقوى الأول ويسلك بالثانى مسلك السداد فاشتق له اسم من النطق، فالمنطق اما مصدر ميمى بمعنى النطق اطلق على العلم المذكور مبالغة فى مدخليته في تكميل النطق حتى كانه هو و اما اسم مكان كان هذا العلم محل النطق و مظهره و في ذكر وجه التسمية اشارة اجمالية الى ما يفضله العلم من المقاصد.
Second: the benefit, and it is what everyone naturally desires to motivate the student and endure the hardship. Third: the name, and it is the title of the science so that he has an overview of what it details. His statement 'third: the name' – 'al-sima' in language means 'mark', and what is intended here is the indication of the reason for naming the science, as it is said: Logic is only called 'mantiq' because 'nutq' (speech) is applied to the outward (which is speech) and to the inward (which is perception of universals), and this science strengthens the former and directs the latter on the path of correctness, so a name was derived for it from 'nutq'. Thus 'mantiq' is either a verbal noun (maṣdar mīmī) meaning 'nutq', applied to the mentioned science as an exaggeration of its role in perfecting 'nutq' until it is as if it itself is it, or a noun of place, meaning this science is the locus and manifestation of 'nutq'. In mentioning the reason for naming, there is a summary indication of the purposes that the science details.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage covers the second and third heads. The second, al-manfa'a (benefit), is defined as that which everyone naturally desires; it motivates the student and helps him endure hardship. The third, al-sima (name), is the title that gives an overview of the science's content. The commentary then expands on the name 'mantiq' for logic. It explains that 'nutq' (speech) has two senses: outward speech (al-nutq al-ẓāhirī) – talking, and inward speech (al-nutq al-bāṭinī) – the perception of universals (ma'ānī kulliyya). The science of logic strengthens the former and guides the latter to correctness. Therefore, the name 'mantiq' is derived from 'nutq'. 'Mantiq' can be interpreted as a verbal noun meaning 'nutq', applied to the science as an exaggeration of its role in perfecting speech, or as a noun of place, indicating that logic is the locus where 'nutq' manifests. This etymology provides a summary indication of the science's purposes. The passage thus links the third head (name) to the actual naming of logic, showing how the name itself conveys the subject matter.

النطق
المنطق
السمة
