The Fourth Head: The Author and the Principle of Evaluating Statements
Author defines fourth head and commentary on evaluating statements vs speakers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the muḥaqqiqūn's principle of evaluating statements by their content rather than their speaker to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Truth is known by its content, not by its speaker — the mark of a verifier (muḥaqqiq).

This passage from a classical logic commentary introduces the fourth head: al-mu'allif (the author). While naming the author settles the student's heart, the commentary quickly elevates the discussion to a key methodological principle: verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) judge statements by their truth, not by who said them. This principle is foundational across all Islamic sciences, from hadith criticism to fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dual purpose of mentioning the author
The matn states the author is mentioned to settle the student's heart — giving confidence. The commentary expands: this is the common way of beginners, who judge statements by the rank of people.
Contrast this with the way of the verifiers.
2
Extract the verifiers' principle
The commentary says 'the verifiers know men by the truth, not the truth by men.' This inverts the common approach. Truth is the criterion; speakers are evaluated based on whether they speak truth.
This principle is captured in the quoted saying: 'Do not look at who said, but look at what was said.'
3
Apply the principle to an Islamic science example
Consider two fatwas: one from a famous scholar but contradicting a clear Quranic text, and one from an unknown student that matches the text. According to the verifiers, which should be accepted? Why?
This tests whether you have internalized the priority of content over speaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: المؤلف ليسكن قلب المتعلم. قوله « الرابع المؤلف ليسكن قلب المتعلم»: على ما هو الشأن في مبادى الحال من معرفة حال الاقوال بمراتب الرجال و اما المحققون فيعرفون الرجال بالحق لا الحق بالرجال و لنعم ما قال ولي ذي الجلال عليه سلام الله المتعال : لا تنظر الى من قال و انظر الى ما قال
Fourth: the author, to settle the student's heart. His statement 'fourth: the author to settle the student's heart': as is the practice in introductory matters of knowing the state of statements by the ranks of men, but the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) know men by the truth, not the truth by men. How excellent is what the friend of majesty, peace and blessings of the Most High be upon him, said: 'Do not look at who said, but look at what was said.'
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage covers the fourth head: al-mu'allif (the author). The purpose of mentioning the author is to settle the student's heart, i.e., to give confidence that the work comes from a reliable source. The commentary then contrasts two approaches. The common approach (or that of the beginners) is to evaluate statements based on the rank of the people who said them. The verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn), however, evaluate the people based on the truth of their statements, not the other way around. The commentary quotes a saying attributed to 'the friend of majesty' (likely a saintly figure): 'Do not look at who said, but look at what was said.' This emphasizes that truth is independent of its speaker, a principle important in the Islamic intellectual tradition where the value of a statement is determined by its content, not the authority of the speaker. This lesson thus has both a practical purpose (naming the author for student reassurance) and a methodological principle (prioritizing content over speaker).

Key Terms
المؤلف (al-mu'allif): The author of the work; mentioned to give the student confidence. [Note: No significant shift.]
المحققون (al-muḥaqqiqūn): The verifiers; those who judge the truth of a statement independently of its speaker. [Note: This term is used broadly in Islamic scholarship to denote expert critical researchers.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) They prefer famous speakers over unknown ones.

Key Takeaway
The verifiers evaluate statements by their truth content, not the speaker's status — a principle captured in the maxim: 'Look at what is said, not who said it.'

Classifying Logic: The Fifth Head and the Definition of Wisdom
Author defines the fifth head (which science) and discusses whether logic is part of wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the reasoning behind classifying logic as a philosophical science or not based on the definition of wisdom.

Core Idea
Whether logic belongs to wisdom depends entirely on how 'wisdom' is defined.

This passage discusses the fifth head of a standard introduction: determining which science a work belongs to. The author asks whether logic is part of the philosophical sciences (al-‘ulūm al-ḥikmiyya). The answer hinges on the definition of wisdom. This classification is not merely academic; it shapes how you approach the study of logic and its relationship to other disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question being asked
The fifth head asks: from which science is this work? The author specifically questions whether logic is from the genus of philosophical sciences (al-‘ulūm al-ḥikmiyya) or not.
2
Examine the two definitions of wisdom
Wisdom (al-ḥikma) is first defined as 'knowledge of the states of the essences of existents as they are in themselves according to human capacity'. This definition includes the word 'essences' (a‘yān). The second definition omits 'essences'.
3
Trace the consequences for logic's classification
Under the first definition, logic is excluded because it only investigates concepts and mental existents, not external essences. Under the second definition, logic is included as part of wisdom.
4
Consider the further subdivision
If logic is part of wisdom, it falls under theoretical wisdom, which studies things beyond human control. Then the question becomes whether logic is a principle (aṣl) of theoretical wisdom or a branch of theology (al-ilāhiyya).
The passage does not resolve this due to space constraints.
5
Reflect on the purpose of classification
Classifying a science correctly guides the seeker to seek what befits it. It also locates the discipline within the broader map of knowledge, showing its relation to other sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: انه من اى علم هو ليطلب فيه ما يليق به. قوله «مسن ای علم :هو :ای من ای جنس من اجناس العلوم ؟ : العقلية او النقلية الفرعية او الاصلية كما يبحث عن حال المنطق انه من جنس العلوم الحكمية ام لا، فان فسرت الحكمة بـ «العلم باحوال اعيان الموجودات على ما هي عليه في نفس الامر بقدر الطاقة البشرية ) ، لم يكن منها ، إذ ليس بحثه الاعن المفهومات والموجودات الذهنية الموصلة الى التصور او الى التصديق، و ان حذف «الاعيان من التفسير المذكورة فهو من الحكمة. ثم على التقدير الثانى فهو من قسم الحكمة النظرية الباحثة عما ليس وجودها بقدرتنا و اختيارنا ، ثم هل هو حينئذ اصل من اصول الحكمة النظرية او من فروع الالهية؟ والمقام لايسع بسط ذلك الكلام
Fifth: which science it is, so that one seeks what befits it. His statement 'from which science' means: from which genus of the genera of sciences? Rational or transmitted, subsidiary or original? As it is investigated regarding the status of logic: is it from the genus of philosophical sciences or not? For if wisdom (ḥikma) is interpreted as 'knowledge of the states of the essences of existents as they are in themselves according to human capacity', then logic is not from it, since its investigation is only about concepts and mental existents that lead to conception (taṣawwur) or assent (taṣdīq). But if 'essences' is omitted from the aforementioned interpretation, then it is from wisdom. Then, on the second assumption, it is from the division of theoretical wisdom, which investigates what whose existence is not within our power and choice. Then, is it then a principle (aṣl) of the principles of theoretical wisdom or a branch of theology (al-ilāhiyya)? The context does not allow expansion of that discussion.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth head: determining which science the work belongs to. The commentary raises the question: from which genus of sciences is logic? Is it rational or transmitted, subsidiary or original? Specifically, is logic part of the philosophical sciences (al-‘ulūm al-ḥikmiyya)? The answer depends on how 'wisdom' (al-ḥikma) is defined. If wisdom is defined as 'knowledge of the states of the essences of external existents as they are in reality according to human capacity', then logic is not part of wisdom because logic only deals with concepts and mental existents that lead to conception or assent. However, if the word 'essences' (a‘yān) is omitted from the definition, then logic can be considered part of wisdom. Under that second definition, logic would then belong to theoretical wisdom, which studies things whose existence is not under our control. The passage then asks whether logic is a principle (aṣl) of theoretical wisdom or a branch of theology (al-ilāhiyya), but it cuts off the discussion due to space. The passage highlights the methodological importance of classifying a science correctly to guide the student in seeking appropriate knowledge.

Key Terms
الحكمة
العلوم الحكمية
المفهومات
الموجودات الذهنية

The Fourth Benefit: Truth by Content, Not Speaker
Explains the fourth benefit of logic with a critical refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of judging statements by their content rather than the authority of the speaker in Islamic scholarly contexts.

Core Idea
Truth is known by the statement, not the speaker.

The fourth benefit of studying logic is learning about the author of a work to settle the learner's heart. However, the commentary immediately refines this: while beginners may need to know the rank of the author, the truly verified scholars judge statements by truth itself, not by the status of the speaker. This lesson unpacks that crucial epistemological principle and its application in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth benefit
Read the first line: 'الرابع: المؤلف ليسكن قلب المتعلم.' The benefit is knowing the author so the student's heart is reassured. Ask yourself: why would knowing the author matter?
2
Contrast between beginners and verifiers
The commentary says beginners look at the rank of men, but verifiers (المحققون) know men by the truth, not truth by men. What does this distinction imply about the relationship between authority and truth?
3
Apply the maxim to an Islamic example
Consider the hadith: 'Do not look at who said, but look at what he said.' (لا تنظر الى من قال و انظر الى ما قال). Apply this to evaluating a fatwa: should you accept it because the scholar is famous, or because the evidence is sound?
This principle is common in usul al-fiqh and jarh wa ta'dil.
4
Reflect on the historical note
The passage mentions Aristotle as the founder of logic. How does knowing this history help, and how could it mislead if one blindly follows authority?

Source Text

Fourth: the author, so that the learner's heart is settled. Fifth: what science is it? So that one may seek in it what is appropriate. He said: 'Fourth: the author...' according to what is usual in the preliminaries of the state of knowing the condition of statements by the ranks of men, but the verifiers know men by the truth, not the truth by men. And excellent is what the Friend of Majesty, peace be upon him, said: 'Do not look at who said, but look at what he said.' The deviser of the laws of logic and philosophy is the great sage Aristotle; he compiled them by order of Alexander, so he was nicknamed the First Teacher, and it was said of logic that it is the inheritance of Dhul-Qarnayn. Then, after the translators transferred those philosophical works from the Greek language to the language of the Arabs, the Second Teacher, the sage Abu Nasr al-Farabi, refined, edited, and perfected them, and after his books were lost, the Chief Master Abu Ali Sina detailed and corrected them—may Allah reward their beautiful efforts. He said: 'From what science is it? It is: from which genus of the genera of sciences? Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and fifth benefits (fawa'id) of studying logic. The fourth benefit is the necessity of knowing the author of a work, so that the student's heart is reassured regarding the reliability of the content. The commentary emphasizes that while beginners need to know the rank of the author, the truly verified scholars judge statements by truth itself, not by the status of the speaker. A quote from an authority reinforces this: look at what is said, not who said it. The historical note traces the development of logic: Aristotle compiled logic at Alexander's command, hence his title 'First Teacher'. Subsequently, al-Farabi translated and refined these works, earning the title 'Second Teacher'. Later, after al-Farabi's books were lost, Avicenna (Ibn Sina) re-detailed and corrected them. The fifth benefit addresses the question of which science logic belongs to. The commentary explores whether logic is part of wisdom (hikma). If wisdom is defined as knowledge of external existents, logic is excluded because it deals with mental concepts and propositions. If the definition is broadened to exclude 'essences', logic can be considered part of wisdom, specifically theoretical wisdom. The passage leaves open whether logic is a fundamental branch of theoretical wisdom or a derivative of theology (ilahiyyat). This discussion grounds logic within the classification of sciences, showing its unique role in providing tools for reasoning rather than studying external reality directly.

المعلم الثاني
الحكمة

Key Takeaway
The fourth benefit teaches that while knowing the author is helpful, the ultimate criterion for truth is the statement itself, not the speaker's authority.

The Fifth Benefit: Classifying Logic Among the Sciences
Explains the fifth benefit of logic with a definitional analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify logic within the Islamic sciences based on its subject matter and evaluate whether it belongs to hikma.

Core Idea
Logic's subject matter—mental concepts—determines its place in the classification of sciences.

The fifth benefit addresses a foundational question: what kind of science is logic? The commentary explores two definitions of hikma (wisdom) and shows that logic's status depends on them. This lesson unpacks the criteria for classifying sciences and applies them to logic, helping you see how Islamic scholars organized knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question of the fifth benefit
Read the line: 'الخامس: انه من اى علم هو ؟ ليطلب فيه ما يليق به.' The question is: to which science does logic belong? This affects what one expects to find in the study of logic.
2
Examine the definition of hikma
The passage gives a definition: 'العلم باحوال اعيان الموجودات على ما هي عليه في نفس الامر بقدر الطاقة البشرية' (knowledge of the states of the essences of existents as they are in themselves). Under this definition, is logic included?
This is the definition from the Peripatetic tradition.
3
Understand why logic is excluded
The commentary says logic is not from hikma because it only investigates 'المفهومات والموجودات الذهنية' (concepts and mental existents) that lead to conception or assent. Why do mental existents fall outside the scope of this definition?
4
Consider the alternative definition
If 'الاعيان' (essences) is omitted from the definition, then logic can be part of hikma. Under that broader definition, hikma includes the study of both external existents and mental concepts. Where would logic fit then?
5
Determine logic's subclass
If logic is part of hikma, it belongs to theoretical wisdom (الحكمة النظرية) because it studies things not subject to our power and choice. The passage then asks: is logic a root (اصل) or a branch (فرع) of theology (الإلهية)? This remains an open question.

The fifth benefit addresses the question of which science logic belongs to. The commentary explores whether logic is part of wisdom (hikma). If wisdom is defined as knowledge of external existents, logic is excluded because it deals with mental concepts and propositions. If the definition is broadened to exclude 'essences', logic can be considered part of wisdom, specifically theoretical wisdom. The passage leaves open whether logic is a fundamental branch of theoretical wisdom or a derivative of theology (ilahiyyat). This discussion grounds logic within the classification of sciences, showing its unique role in providing tools for reasoning rather than studying external reality directly.

Key Terms
المعلم الأول
المعلم الثاني
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, logic is definitely part of hikma.
B) No, because logic deals with mental concepts, not external essences.
C) Yes, because logic is a tool for all sciences.
D) Only if we add 'essences' back into the definition.

Key Takeaway
The fifth benefit shows that logic's classification depends on how we define its subject matter; it is either a part of hikma (with a broader definition) or a distinct instrumental science.

The Proper Sequence for Studying Logic
Author discusses the proper sequence of studying logic after ethics and geometry, or after literary sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the recommended order for studying logic and justify the adaptation of that order based on student preparedness.

Core Idea
Logic is studied after ethics and geometry, but in Arabic contexts, prioritize literary sciences first.

The study of logic, like any discipline, requires proper sequencing. This lesson examines where logic fits in a classical Islamic curriculum. The author advises that logic should come after moral refinement and mental training in geometry. However, a later adaptation shifts this order to prioritize Arabic literary sciences, reflecting the needs of a linguistically diverse scholarly community.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two recommended sequences
Locate the two sequences mentioned: the classical sequence (ethics then geometry then logic) and the contemporary sequence (literary sciences then logic). Note the order of each.
2
Analyze the rationale for each sequence
Why is ethics foundational? Because moral refinement prepares the student. Why geometry? It trains the mind in precise reasoning. Why literary sciences? Arabic is the language of scholarship.
Consider the shift from intellectual to linguistic preparation.
3
Compare the two sequences
What factors cause the change? The prevalence of Arabic writings in the scholar's time. The earlier sequence assumed a different linguistic context.
This shows curriculum adaptation to circumstances.
4
Apply the principle to your own study
Determine what you need before studying logic: Arabic proficiency, ethical grounding, or geometric reasoning? Justify your order.
Reflect on the needs of your own background.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: انه في اى مرتبة، هو؟ ليقدم على ما يجب ويؤخر عما يجب. قوله فى اى مرتبة هو كما يقال : ان مرتبة المنطق ان يشتغل به بعد تهذيب الاخلاق وتقويم الفكر ببعض الهندسيات. وذكر الاستاد في بعض رسائله : انه ينبغى تأخيره فى زماننا هذا عن ان يعلم قدر صالح من العلوم الادبية لما شاع من كون التداوين باللغة العربية.
Sixth: In what rank is it? So that it may be placed before what is necessary and after what is necessary. He said: 'In what rank is it?' as it is said: The rank of logic is that one occupies oneself with it after refinement of ethics and correction of thought through some geometry. And the teacher mentioned in some of his epistles that it should be delayed in our time until after one learns a sufficient amount of literary sciences, because of the prevalence of writings being in the Arabic language.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage addresses the sixth benefit of logic: determining its proper place in the curriculum. The matn states that logic should be studied after what is necessary before it and before what comes after. The commentary explains that in earlier times, the recommended order was to first purify one's ethics and then train the mind with geometry before studying logic. Geometry disciplines the intellect and prevents confusion. However, the teacher (likely the author of the original matn or a later authority) notes that in contemporary times (of the hashiya author), it is better to delay logic until after acquiring a sufficient grounding in Arabic literary sciences (adab). This is because most scientific writings are in Arabic, and without that linguistic foundation, the student cannot properly engage with the texts. Thus, the order of study is adaptable to circumstances.

تقويم الفكر
العلوم الادبية

The Proper Rank of Logic Among Sciences
Author discusses the rank of logic among the sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the proper order of studying logic relative to other sciences based on the author's criteria of ethical, geometrical, and linguistic preparation.

Core Idea
The rank of logic is after ethics and geometry but before theology, and in later times after Arabic literature.

This lesson focuses on the sixth rule for organizing a science: determining its proper rank (marṯaba). The author discusses where logic should be placed in the curriculum, emphasizing the need for ethical refinement, geometrical training, and in later times, competence in Arabic literary sciences. Understanding this order helps structure a coherent educational path.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two criteria for ranking logic
First, logic should be studied after refining morals and training thought with geometry. Second, in the author's time, it should be delayed until sufficient Arabic literary sciences are learned because textbooks are in Arabic.
Both criteria reflect practical and pedagogical considerations.
2
Understand why logic follows these preparatory sciences
Ethical refinement prevents misuse of logic, geometry sharpens reasoning, and Arabic proficiency ensures comprehension of texts. Logic is the tool for higher sciences like kalam.
3
Apply the ranking principle to other sciences
Consider the modern curriculum: what preparatory subjects should precede a new course? Use the same reasoning: foundational skills first, then advanced tools, then specialized fields.
This principle is not absolute but context-dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اجزاء العلوم السادس: انه في اى مرتبة، هو؟ ليقدم على ما يجب ويؤخر عما يجب. السابع: القسمة ليطلب في كل باب ما يليق به. الثامن: الانحاء التعليمية. قوله فى اى مرتبة هو كما يقال : ان مرتبة المنطق ان يشتغل به بعد تهذيب الاخلاق وتقويم الفكر ببعض الهندسيات. وذكر الاستاد في بعض رسائله : انه ينبغى تأخيره فى زماننا هذا عن ان يعلم قدر صالح من العلوم الادبية لما شاع من كون التداوين باللغة العربية. قوله « القسمة»: اى قسمة العلم او الكتاب الى ابوابهما. فالاول كما يقال ابواب المنطق تسعة الاول: باب إيساغوجي ای الكليات الخمس. الثاني: التعريفات الثالث القضايا. الرابع: القياس و اخواه الخامس البرهان السادس الجدل السابع: الخطابة الثامن: المغالطة. التاسع: الشعر. و بعضهم عد بحث الالفاظ باباً آخر فعاد أبواب المنطق عشرة كاملة. والثانى كما يقال ان كتابنا هذا مرتب على قسمين: القسم الأول في المنطق و هو مرتب على مقدمة و مقصدين وخاتمة المقدمة فى بيان الماهية والغاية و الموضوع ، المقصد الأول فى مباحث التصورات، المقصد الثاني في مباحث التصديقات الخاتمة فى اجزاء العلوم القسم الثانى فى علم الكلام وهو مرتب على كذا ،ابواب الاول في كذا الخ و كما قال فى الشمسية ورتبته على مقدمة وثلاث مقالات وخاتمة، وهذا الثانى شائع كثير فلا يخلو عنه كتاب. قوله « الانحاء التعليمية» اى الطرق المذكورة في التعاليم لعموم نفعها في العلوم وقد اضطربت كلمة الشراح هيهنا و ما نذكره هو الموافق لتتبع كتب القوم و المأخوذ من شرح المطالع . الانحاء جمع نحو وهو الطريق، وقد يجىء لمعان آخر ذكرت في بعض كتب النحو و البيت الجامع لها مشهور ثم الانحاء اربعة الاول: التقسيم والثانى: التحليل و الثالث: التحديد و الرابع البرهان و ستذكر على التفصيل .
On the parts of the sciences. Sixth: In what rank is it? It is to be presented before what must be and delayed after what must be. Seventh: Division (al-qisma) is to seek in each chapter what is suitable to it. Eighth: The instructional methods (al-inḥāʾ al-taʿlīmiyya). His saying: 'In what rank is it' — it is said that the rank of logic is that one should occupy himself with it after the refinement of morals and the rectification of thought by some geometrical sciences. And the teacher mentioned in some of his epistles that it should be delayed in our time until one learns a sufficient amount of the literary sciences because of the prevalence of compositions being in the Arabic language. His saying: 'The division' — i.e., the division of the science or the book into its chapters. As for the first, it is said that the chapters of logic are nine: the first, the chapter of Isagoge, i.e., the five universals; the second, definitions; the third, propositions; the fourth, syllogism and its two companions; the fifth, demonstration; the sixth, dialectic; the seventh, rhetoric; the eighth, sophistry; the ninth, poetry. And some counted the investigation of terms as another chapter, so the chapters of logic became a complete ten. As for the second, it is said that our book is arranged into two sections: the first section on logic, and it is arranged into an introduction, two aims, and a conclusion — the introduction on the essence, purpose, and subject; the first aim on the investigations of concepts; the second aim on the investigations of assents; the conclusion on the parts of the sciences. The second section on theology, and it is arranged into such-and-such — chapters, first on such-and-such, etc. And as he said in al-Shamsiyya: 'And he arranged it into an introduction, three articles, and a conclusion.' And this second is common and frequent, so no book is devoid of it. His saying: 'The instructional methods' — i.e., the methods mentioned in the teachings for the generality of their benefit in the sciences. And the words of the commentators have become confused here, and what we mention is in agreement with the investigation of the works of the folk and is taken from Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ. Al-inḥāʾ is the plural of naḥw, meaning path, and it comes for other meanings mentioned in some books of grammar, and the verse encompassing them is well-known. Then the methods are four: first, division; second, analysis; third, definition; fourth, demonstration, and they will be mentioned in detail.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory commentary on the parts of sciences, specifically regarding logic. It begins by addressing three rules for organizing a science: the sixth rule concerns the proper order (maṟtaba) of a science — logic should be studied after refining morals and training thought with geometry, but in the author's time it should be delayed until sufficient Arabic literary sciences are learned. The seventh rule is about division (qisma), meaning to divide the science or book into appropriate chapters. The author then lists two examples of division: first, the classic enumeration of the nine (or ten) chapters of logic, starting with Isagoge (the five universals), definitions, propositions, syllogism with its two companions (probably induction and analogy), demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, sophistry, poetry, and some add a chapter on terms. Second, the division of the author's own book into two main sections: logic and theology, with further subdivisions. The eighth rule is about the instructional methods (al-inḥāʾ al-taʿlīmiyya), which are four: division (taqsīm), analysis (taḥlīl), definition (taḥdīd), and demonstration (burhān). These methods are taken from Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ and are explained in detail later. The commentary clarifies that the term 'inḥāʾ' is plural of 'naḥw' (way, path) and has other meanings in grammar. The key takeaway is that the structure of a science textbook should follow these rules: proper ordering, appropriate division into chapters, and employing the four instructional methods for teaching.

Key Terms
المرتبة
المنطق
تهذيب الاخلاق
العلوم الهندسية
العلوم الادبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Ethics and geometry only
B) Ethics, geometry, and Arabic literature
C) Arabic literature alone
D) Theology and jurisprudence

Key Takeaway
The rank of logic is not absolute; it depends on the student's preparation and the linguistic context of the texts.

Division of a Science or Book into Chapters
Author defines and exemplifies division of science/book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to divide a science or textbook into its proper chapters following the examples given for logic and for the author's own book.

Core Idea
Dividing a science into chapters is essential for systematic learning and teaching.

This lesson covers the seventh rule of science organization: division (qisma). The author defines it as dividing the science or the book into chapters and provides two detailed examples: the classic nine (or ten) chapters of logic and the division of his own work into sections on logic and kalam. Understanding these divisions helps in structuring any discipline.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of division
Division can be of a science (e.g., chapters of logic) or of a book (e.g., sections of a textbook). Both follow similar principles of partitioning content logically.
The author lists both to show application.
2
Memorize the nine (or ten) chapters of logic
The chapters are: 1) Isagoge (five universals), 2) Definitions, 3) Propositions, 4) Syllogism and its two companions, 5) Demonstration, 6) Dialectic, 7) Rhetoric, 8) Sophistry, 9) Poetry. Some add a tenth on terms. This order reflects the progression from concepts to proofs to types of argumentation.
The 'two companions' refer to induction and analogy, which are often discussed alongside syllogism.
3
Analyze the division of the author's book
The book is divided into two main sections: logic and kalam. The logic section has an introduction (essence, purpose, subject), two aims (concepts and assents), and a conclusion (parts of sciences). The kalam section follows a similar pattern. This shows how a book can mirror the structure of the science.
4
Practice dividing another Islamic science into chapters
Take a science like usul al-fiqh and propose its main chapters (e.g., commands, prohibitions, general and specific, etc.) based on the logical progression from concepts to rulings.
Refer to standard textbooks for comparison.

The Four Instructional Methods: Division, Analysis, Definition, Demonstration
Author enumerates and defines the four instructional methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to name the four instructional methods, define each, and identify which method is being used in a given teaching scenario from the Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The four instructional methods are the pedagogical tools for teaching any science effectively.

This lesson introduces the eighth rule: the instructional methods (al-inḥāʾ al-taʿlīmiyya). The author lists four: division (taqsīm), analysis (taḥlīl), definition (taḥdīd), and demonstration (burhān). These methods are not chapters of logic but approaches to teaching that apply across sciences. Understanding them helps in both learning and teaching Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
List the four methods and their Arabic names
Memorize: التقسيم (taqsīm – division), التحليل (taḥlīl – analysis), التحديد (taḥdīd – definition), البرهان (burhān – demonstration).
The plural is الانحاء, singular نحو meaning way.
2
Define each method
Division: dividing a genus into species or a whole into parts. Analysis: breaking a compound into its elements or a syllogism into premises. Definition: delimiting a concept by its genus and differentia. Demonstration: proving a conclusion through a certain syllogism.
These definitions are based on later explanations, not given in detail here.
3
Understand how each method is used in teaching logic
In teaching logic, division is used to present the chapters; analysis to break down a syllogism; definition to clarify terms; demonstration to prove validity. The author says these will be detailed later.
4
Practice identifying the method in a sample teaching passage
Given a passage from a fiqh or kalam textbook, identify whether the teacher is using division (e.g., listing types of water), analysis (e.g., breaking down an obligation into conditions), definition (e.g., defining istinja), or demonstration (e.g., proving a ruling from evidence).
Refer to real texts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اجزاء العلوم السادس: انه في اى مرتبة، هو؟ ليقدم على ما يجب ويؤخر عما يجب. السابع: القسمة ليطلب في كل باب ما يليق به. الثامن: الانحاء التعليمية. قوله فى اى مرتبة هو كما يقال : ان مرتبة المنطق ان يشتغل به بعد تهذيب الاخلاق وتقويم الفكر ببعض الهندسيات. وذكر الاستاد في بعض رسائله : انه ينبغى تأخيره فى زماننا هذا عن ان يعلم قدر صالح من العلوم الادبية لما شاع من كون التداوين باللغة العربية. قوله « القسمة»: اى قسمة العلم او الكتاب الى ابوابهما. فالاول كما يقال ابواب المنطق تسعة الاول: باب إيساغوجي ای الكليات الخمس. الثاني: التعريفات الثالث القضايا. الرابع: القياس و اخواه الخامس البرهان السادس الجدل السابع: الخطابة الثامن: المغالطة. التاسع: الشعر. و بعضهم عد بحث الالفاظ باباً آخر فعاد أبواب المنطق عشرة كاملة. والثانى كما يقال ان كتابنا هذا مرتب على قسمين: القسم الأول في المنطق و هو مرتب على مقدمة و مقصدين وخاتمة المقدمة فى بيان الماهية والغاية و الموضوع ، المقصد الأول فى مباحث التصورات، المقصد الثاني في مباحث التصديقات الخاتمة فى اجزاء العلوم القسم الثانى فى علم الكلام وهو مرتب على كذا ،ابواب الاول في كذا الخ و كما قال فى الشمسية ورتبته على مقدمة وثلاث مقالات وخاتمة، وهذا الثانى شائع كثير فلا يخلو عنه كتاب. قوله « الانحاء التعليمية» اى الطرق المذكورة في التعاليم لعموم نفعها في العلوم وقد اضطربت كلمة الشراح هيهنا و ما نذكره هو الموافق لتتبع كتب القوم و المأخوذ من شرح المطالع . الانحاء جمع نحو وهو الطريق، وقد يجىء لمعان آخر ذكرت في بعض كتب النحو و البيت الجامع لها مشهور ثم الانحاء اربعة الاول: التقسيم والثانى: التحليل و الثالث: التحديد و الرابع البرهان و ستذكر على التفصيل .
On the parts of the sciences. Sixth: In what rank is it? It is to be presented before what must be and delayed after what must be. Seventh: Division (al-qisma) is to seek in each chapter what is suitable to it. Eighth: The instructional methods (al-inḥāʾ al-taʿlīmiyya). His saying: 'In what rank is it' — it is said that the rank of logic is that one should occupy himself with it after the refinement of morals and the rectification of thought by some geometrical sciences. And the teacher mentioned in some of his epistles that it should be delayed in our time until one learns a sufficient amount of the literary sciences because of the prevalence of compositions being in the Arabic language. His saying: 'The division' — i.e., the division of the science or the book into its chapters. As for the first, it is said that the chapters of logic are nine: the first, the chapter of Isagoge, i.e., the five universals; the second, definitions; the third, propositions; the fourth, syllogism and its two companions; the fifth, demonstration; the sixth, dialectic; the seventh, rhetoric; the eighth, sophistry; the ninth, poetry. And some counted the investigation of terms as another chapter, so the chapters of logic became a complete ten. As for the second, it is said that our book is arranged into two sections: the first section on logic, and it is arranged into an introduction, two aims, and a conclusion — the introduction on the essence, purpose, and subject; the first aim on the investigations of concepts; the second aim on the investigations of assents; the conclusion on the parts of the sciences. The second section on theology, and it is arranged into such-and-such — chapters, first on such-and-such, etc. And as he said in al-Shamsiyya: 'And he arranged it into an introduction, three articles, and a conclusion.' And this second is common and frequent, so no book is devoid of it. His saying: 'The instructional methods' — i.e., the methods mentioned in the teachings for the generality of their benefit in the sciences. And the words of the commentators have become confused here, and what we mention is in agreement with the investigation of the works of the folk and is taken from Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ. Al-inḥāʾ is the plural of naḥw, meaning path, and it comes for other meanings mentioned in some books of grammar, and the verse encompassing them is well-known. Then the methods are four: first, division; second, analysis; third, definition; fourth, demonstration, and they will be mentioned in detail.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory commentary on the parts of sciences, specifically regarding logic. It begins by addressing three rules for organizing a science: the sixth rule concerns the proper order (maṟtaba) of a science — logic should be studied after refining morals and training thought with geometry, but in the author's time it should be delayed until sufficient Arabic literary sciences are learned. The seventh rule is about division (qisma), meaning to divide the science or book into appropriate chapters. The author then lists two examples of division: first, the classic enumeration of the nine (or ten) chapters of logic, starting with Isagoge (the five universals), definitions, propositions, syllogism with its two companions (probably induction and analogy), demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, sophistry, poetry, and some add a chapter on terms. Second, the division of the author's own book into two main sections: logic and theology, with further subdivisions. The eighth rule is about the instructional methods (al-inḥāʾ al-taʿlīmiyya), which are four: division (taqsīm), analysis (taḥlīl), definition (taḥdīd), and demonstration (burhān). These methods are taken from Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ and are explained in detail later. The commentary clarifies that the term 'inḥāʾ' is plural of 'naḥw' (way, path) and has other meanings in grammar. The key takeaway is that the structure of a science textbook should follow these rules: proper ordering, appropriate division into chapters, and employing the four instructional methods for teaching.

Introduction and Front Matter

Orthography and History of the Basmala
Explains the spelling and history of the basmala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the orthographic convention of omitting the alif in 'bism' and trace its historical development from pre-Islamic times to the final prescription.

Core Idea
The basmala's orthography reflects three revelations and a counterintuitive scribal rule.

The basmala is more than a phrase; its spelling carries layers of meaning. In this lesson, we analyze why the alif is dropped and how the Prophet's (s) instruction evolved through revelations from Surah Hud, al-Isra, and an-Naml.

Guided Path
1
Identify the orthographic anomaly
Notice that 'bism' is written without the alif of 'ism'. This is contrary to standard Arabic spelling rules.
The alif is omitted in the phrase 'bismi llāh' due to frequent usage.
2
Trace the historical narrative
List the stages: Quraysh wrote 'bismika llāhumma'; after Surah Hud (41) the Prophet commanded 'bismi llāh'; after al-Isra (110) added 'ar-rahmān'; after an-Naml (30) added 'ar-rahīm'.
Each stage added a divine name.
3
Understand the compensatory lengthening
The letter bā' is lengthened (madd) to compensate for the omitted alif. This is a known scribal practice in rasm al-mushaf.
The lengthened bā' is written as 'بِسْـ' with a shaddah-like elongation.
4
Connect to practice
The basmala is considered a verse from every sūra except Sūrat at-Tawba. This ruling is derived from the traditions mentioned.
This affects how the Qur'an is recited and written.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل الوجه فى كتابة البسملة بحذف الألف على خلاف وضع الخط كثرة الاستعمال، وتطويل الباء عوض عنها. روي ان قريشاً كانت تكتب في الجاهلية «بسمك اللهم» حتى نزلت سورة هود فيها «بسم مجراها و مرساها» فامر النبي (ص) ان يكتب «بسم الله» ثم نزل عليه بعد ذلك «قل ادعوا الله او ادعوا الرحمن أيا ما تدعوا فله الأسماء الحسنى» فامر صلى الله عليه و آله ان يكتب «بسم الله الرحمن» فلما نزلت سورة النمل « إنه من سليمان و إنه بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم» امر (ص) ان يكتب ذلك في صدور الكتب و اوائل الرسائل و هي آية من كل سورة. و قولنا «بسم الله» اي ابتدأ بسم الله او ابتدائي بسم الله او ابدأوا بسم الله فهو خير مبتدأ محذوف. او استعين او آيم. و اشتقاق الاسم من السمو و هو العلو والرفعة و منه سما الزرع اى علا وارتفع ومنه اشتقاق السماء لارتفاعها وعلوها وقيل هو مشتق من السمة التى هى العلامة فكانه علامة لما وضع له. قول المصنف (الحمد لله) ... انما عدل عن الفعلية الى الاسمية، دلالة على الثبات و الدوام و اقتضاء لكلام الملك العلام، وقدم الحمد لمزيد الاهتمام به بمقتضى المقام وان كان ذكر الله اهم في نفسه فإن الاسمية بحسب الحال اقوى منها بحسب الذات و لذا لم يقدم في قوله تعالى: «فلله الحمد رب السموات و له الحمد في السموات والارض» فان الغرض الاصلي هنالك بيان اختصاصه تعالى بالحمد لا اثبات الحمد له.
It was said: The reason for writing the basmala with the omission of the alif contrary to orthographic convention is frequent usage, and the lengthening of the ba' compensates for it. It is narrated that Quraysh used to write in pre-Islamic times 'In Your name, O Allah' until the Surah Hud was revealed, in which there is 'In the name of its course and its anchorage.' So the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded to write 'In the name of Allah.' Then after that was revealed to him 'Say: Call upon Allah or call upon the Most Merciful; whichever you call, to Him belong the Most Beautiful Names.' So he commanded to write 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious.' Then when Surah an-Naml was revealed, 'Indeed, it is from Solomon, and indeed it is: In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful,' he commanded to write that at the beginning of books and at the start of letters, and it is a verse from every surah. And our saying 'In the name of Allah' means: I begin with the name of Allah, or my beginning is in the name of Allah, or begin with the name of Allah, so it is a good beginning with an omitted subject. Or: I seek help, or I ask. And the derivation of 'name' is from 'sama' which means height and elevation, from which 'crop rises high' (sama al-zar') meaning it grew high; and from it is derived 'sky' (sama') because of its height and elevation. And it is said it is derived from 'sima' which is the mark, so it is as if it is a mark for that which it is named. The author's saying 'Praise be to Allah' ... he only turned from the verbal form to the nominal form to indicate permanence and continuity and requirement for the speech of the King, the All-Knowing. And he prefaced praise due to greater concern for it by context, even though the mention of Allah is more important in itself, for the nominal form is stronger in terms of context than in terms of essence. Hence it was not prefaced in His saying: 'To Allah belongs praise, Lord of the heavens, and to Him belongs praise in the heavens and the earth' for the primary purpose there is to show specificity of praise to Him, not to establish praise for Him.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hāshiya) on the opening of a book, discussing two main elements: the basmala and the hamdalah. 

The first part addresses the orthography of the basmala. It explains that the alif of 'ism' is omitted in writing due to frequent usage, and the lengthened bā' compensates. A historical overview is given: Quraysh originally wrote 'bismika llāhumma'. After Sūrat Hūd (verse 41) mentioned 'bismi majrāhā wa mursāhā', the Prophet instructed to write 'bismi llāh'. Later, with the revelation of 'qul id'ū llāha aw id'ū r-rahmān' (al-Isrā' 110), 'bismi llāhi r-rahmān' was added. Finally, with Sūrat an-Naml (verse 30), the full 'bismi llāhi r-rahmāni r-rahīm' was prescribed for letters and book beginnings. The basmala is considered a verse from every sūra (except Sūrat at-Tawba). 

Grammatically, 'bismillāh' is analyzed as a sentence with an omitted predicate (or verb), meaning 'I begin with the name of Allah', or 'my beginning is with the name of Allah', or 'begin with the name of Allah'. Alternative interpretations view it as 'I seek help' or 'I ask'. 

The derivation of 'ism' is discussed: from 's-m-w' meaning height, as in crops rising and the sky's elevation; or from 's-m-y' meaning a mark, as a name marks its referent. 

The second part concerns the author's choice of the nominal phrase 'al-hamdu lillāh' over a verbal form (e.g., 'ahmadu llāha'). The shift to nominal indicates permanence and continuity, befitting the King, the All-Knowing. Praise is placed before the name of Allah to show greater concern for praise due to context, even though Allah's name is more important in itself. The nominal form is contextually stronger. This is contrasted with the Qur'ānic verse 'fa-lillāhi l-ḥamdu rabbi s-samāwāti' (al-Jāthiya 36), where praise is delayed because the purpose is to specify that praise belongs solely to Allah, not to establish praise.

Key Terms
بسملة
اسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what was the Prophet's command after the revelation of Surah Hud?
A) To write 'bismika llāhumma'
B) To write 'bismi llāh'
C) To write 'bismi llāhi r-rahmān'
D) To write 'bismi llāhi r-rahmāni r-rahīm'

Key Takeaway
The orthographic omission of alif in the basmala is an intentional convention due to frequent use, and its current form was established through a series of revelations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine a copy of the Qur'an and identify three other places where alif is omitted in the Uthmanic script (e.g., 'bismi rabbika' or 'ilaha'). Explain the reason based on the same principle of frequent usage.

Derivation of 'Ism' and Grammar of Basmala
Explains the derivation of 'ism' and the grammar of basmala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two proposed derivations of the word 'ism' and analyse the grammatical structure of 'bismillah' as a sentence with an omitted predicate.

Core Idea
The word 'ism' signifies either height or a mark, and 'bismillah' is a sentence meaning 'I begin with Allah's name'.

The basmala is not just a phrase; it is a complete utterance with a deep linguistic root. In this lesson, we explore the etymology of 'ism' and the various grammatical interpretations of the phrase.

Guided Path
1
Examine the derivation from sumuww
Identify the root s-m-w meaning height or elevation. Examples: 'sama al-zar'' (the crop rose high), 'sama'' (sky).
This derivation connects the name to its elevated meaning.
2
Examine the derivation from sima
Identify the root s-m-y meaning a mark or sign. A name is a mark for its referent.
This derivation emphasizes the function of naming.
3
Parse the phrase 'bismillah'
Break down: bā (preposition) + ism (noun, with omitted alif) + allah (proper noun). The bā has meanings of beginning or seeking help.
The omission of alif is orthographic, not grammatical.
4
Identify the omitted predicate
The phrase is a sentence with an omitted subject or verb. Common interpretations include: 'I begin with the name of Allah', 'my beginning is in the name of Allah', or 'I seek help through the name of Allah'.
Each interpretation carries a different emphasis.

Introduction and Front Matter

The Nominal Form of Hamdalah: Permanence and Context
Explains the choice of nominal over verbal in hamdalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author chose 'al-hamdu lillah' over a verbal form and how this differs from the Qur'anic verse 'fa-lillahi al-hamd'.

Core Idea
Nominal forms imply permanence, while verbal forms imply temporality; context determines which is stronger.

A subtle rhetorical choice lies in the shift from 'ahmadu llah' to 'al-hamdu lillah'. In this lesson, we unpack the grammatical significance of the nominal form and its contextual strength.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two forms
Verbal: 'ahmadu llaha' (I praise Allah) is a finite verb indicating a single act. Nominal: 'al-hamdu lillah' (Praise is for Allah) is a nominal sentence expressing a permanent state.
The nominal form uses a definite noun (al-hamdu) and a prepositional phrase (lillah).
2
Understand the semantic implication
The commentator states that the nominal form indicates permanence (thabat) and continuity (dawam). It is stronger in establishing the constant nature of praise.
This is a principle of Arabic rhetoric (balaghah).
3
Analyze the placement
The author placed 'al-hamdu' before 'lillah' to show greater concern for praise given the context, even though the name of Allah is more important in itself.
The principle: contextual strength (quwwat al-hal) can outweigh essential strength (quwwat al-dhat).
4
Apply the principle to Qur'anic verse
In 'fa-lillahi al-hamd' (al-Jathiya 36), the name is fronted because the purpose is to specify that praise belongs solely to Allah, not to establish praise itself.
The rhetorical goal determines the word order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل الوجه فى كتابة البسملة بحذف الألف على خلاف وضع الخط كثرة الاستعمال، وتطويل الباء عوض عنها. روي ان قريشاً كانت تكتب في الجاهلية «بسمك اللهم» حتى نزلت سورة هود فيها «بسم مجراها و مرساها» فامر النبي (ص) ان يكتب «بسم الله» ثم نزل عليه بعد ذلك «قل ادعوا الله او ادعوا الرحمن أيا ما تدعوا فله الأسماء الحسنى» فامر صلى الله عليه و آله ان يكتب «بسم الله الرحمن» فلما نزلت سورة النمل « إنه من سليمان و إنه بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم» امر (ص) ان يكتب ذلك في صدور الكتب و اوائل الرسائل و هي آية من كل سورة. و قولنا «بسم الله» اي ابتدأ بسم الله او ابتدائي بسم الله او ابدأوا بسم الله فهو خير مبتدأ محذوف. او استعين او آيم. و اشتقاق الاسم من السمو و هو العلو والرفعة و منه سما الزرع اى علا وارتفع ومنه اشتقاق السماء لارتفاعها وعلوها وقيل هو مشتق من السمة التى هى العلامة فكانه علامة لما وضع له. قول المصنف (الحمد لله) ... انما عدل عن الفعلية الى الاسمية، دلالة على الثبات و الدوام و اقتضاء لكلام الملك العلام، وقدم الحمد لمزيد الاهتمام به بمقتضى المقام وان كان ذكر الله اهم في نفسه فإن الاسمية بحسب الحال اقوى منها بحسب الذات و لذا لم يقدم في قوله تعالى: «فلله الحمد رب السموات و له الحمد في السموات والارض» فان الغرض الاصلي هنالك بيان اختصاصه تعالى بالحمد لا اثبات الحمد له.
It was said: The reason for writing the basmala with the omission of the alif contrary to orthographic convention is frequent usage, and the lengthening of the ba' compensates for it. It is narrated that Quraysh used to write in pre-Islamic times 'In Your name, O Allah' until the Surah Hud was revealed, in which there is 'In the name of its course and its anchorage.' So the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded to write 'In the name of Allah.' Then after that was revealed to him 'Say: Call upon Allah or call upon the Most Merciful; whichever you call, to Him belong the Most Beautiful Names.' So he commanded to write 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious.' Then when Surah an-Naml was revealed, 'Indeed, it is from Solomon, and indeed it is: In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful,' he commanded to write that at the beginning of books and at the start of letters, and it is a verse from every surah. And our saying 'In the name of Allah' means: I begin with the name of Allah, or my beginning is in the name of Allah, or begin with the name of Allah, so it is a good beginning with an omitted subject. Or: I seek help, or I ask. And the derivation of 'name' is from 'sama' which means height and elevation, from which 'crop rises high' (sama al-zar') meaning it grew high; and from it is derived 'sky' (sama') because of its height and elevation. And it is said it is derived from 'sima' which is the mark, so it is as if it is a mark for that which it is named. The author's saying 'Praise be to Allah' ... he only turned from the verbal form to the nominal form to indicate permanence and continuity and requirement for the speech of the King, the All-Knowing. And he prefaced praise due to greater concern for it by context, even though the mention of Allah is more important in itself, for the nominal form is stronger in terms of context than in terms of essence. Hence it was not prefaced in His saying: 'To Allah belongs praise, Lord of the heavens, and to Him belongs praise in the heavens and the earth' for the primary purpose there is to show specificity of praise to Him, not to establish praise for Him.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hāshiya) on the opening of a book, discussing two main elements: the basmala and the hamdalah. 

The first part addresses the orthography of the basmala. It explains that the alif of 'ism' is omitted in writing due to frequent usage, and the lengthened bā' compensates. A historical overview is given: Quraysh originally wrote 'bismika llāhumma'. After Sūrat Hūd (verse 41) mentioned 'bismi majrāhā wa mursāhā', the Prophet instructed to write 'bismi llāh'. Later, with the revelation of 'qul id'ū llāha aw id'ū r-rahmān' (al-Isrā' 110), 'bismi llāhi r-rahmān' was added. Finally, with Sūrat an-Naml (verse 30), the full 'bismi llāhi r-rahmāni r-rahīm' was prescribed for letters and book beginnings. The basmala is considered a verse from every sūra (except Sūrat at-Tawba). 

Grammatically, 'bismillāh' is analyzed as a sentence with an omitted predicate (or verb), meaning 'I begin with the name of Allah', or 'my beginning is with the name of Allah', or 'begin with the name of Allah'. Alternative interpretations view it as 'I seek help' or 'I ask'. 

The derivation of 'ism' is discussed: from 's-m-w' meaning height, as in crops rising and the sky's elevation; or from 's-m-y' meaning a mark, as a name marks its referent. 

The second part concerns the author's choice of the nominal phrase 'al-hamdu lillāh' over a verbal form (e.g., 'ahmadu llāha'). The shift to nominal indicates permanence and continuity, befitting the King, the All-Knowing. Praise is placed before the name of Allah to show greater concern for praise due to context, even though Allah's name is more important in itself. The nominal form is contextually stronger. This is contrasted with the Qur'ānic verse 'fa-lillāhi l-ḥamdu rabbi s-samāwāti' (al-Jāthiya 36), where praise is delayed because the purpose is to specify that praise belongs solely to Allah, not to establish praise.

Key Terms
حمد
فعلية
اسمية

Key Takeaway
The choice of nominal over verbal in the hamdalah is a rhetorical decision to convey permanence, and word order reflects contextual priority.

Introduction and Front Matter

The Basmala: Orthography, Grammar, and Derivation
Commentator explains the reason for omitting the alif in basmala and quotes sources on its historical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the orthographic, grammatical, and philological reasons for the basmala's form as given in traditional commentaries.

Core Idea
The basmala's form is not arbitrary; it is grounded in frequent usage, revealed verses, and systematic grammar.

This lesson explores the commentator's expansion on the basmala, the opening phrase of the Quran. You will learn why the alif is omitted, how the basmala was historically revealed, and the grammatical parsing of 'bismi'. Additionally, the derivation of the word 'ism' is discussed. Understanding these details is crucial for correctly appreciating the basmala in Quranic and Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the orthographic peculiarity
Note that the basmala is written 'بسم' without an alif, unlike the standard spelling of 'اسم'. The commentator attributes this to frequent usage (kathrat al-isti'mal) and a lengthened ba' compensating.
This is a common feature in Quranic orthography.
2
Trace the historical revelation
Follow the account of how the basmala evolved from the Jahiliyya practice ('bismika allahumma') to the final form 'bismillah al-rahman al-rahim' through successive Quranic verses (Hud 11:41, al-Isra 17:110, al-Naml 27:30).
The basmala was revealed in stages, each adding more elements.
3
Analyze the grammatical parsing
Examine the two views on the grammatical status of 'bismi': one as an accusative object of an implied verb (like 'abda'u' or 'asta'inu'), another as a nominative subject of a deleted predicate (e.g., 'ibtida'i bismi Allah'). The deletion is allowed due to contextual indication.
The grammatical analysis affects the meaning of the basmala.

(مجمع البيان ج ۱ ص ۲۰) او استعين او آيم (تفسير سورة الحمد والبقرة للاستاد الشهيد مطهري ص (۱۲) و...] و اشتقاق الاسم من السمو و هو العلو والرفعة و منه سما الزرع اى علا وارتفع ومنه اشتقاق السماء لارتفاعها وعلوها وقيل هو مشتق من السمة التى هى العلامة فكانه علامة لما وضع له (تفسير الصافي ج اص ٥٠ طبع الاسلامية)
It is said the reason in writing the basmala with the omission of the alif, contrary to the orthographic norm, is frequent usage, and the lengthening of the ba' is a substitute for it. Allah—it is narrated that Quraysh used to write in the Jahiliyya 'bismika allahumma' until Surah Hud was revealed, in which is 'bismi majreha wa mursaha', so the Prophet (PBUH) ordered to write 'bismi Allah'. Then after that was revealed 'Say: Call upon Allah or call upon al-Rahman, whichever you call, to Him belong the most beautiful names', so he ordered to write 'bismi Allah al-Rahman'. When Surah al-Naml was revealed 'Indeed it is from Solomon and indeed it is bismi Allah al-Rahman al-Raheem', he ordered to write that at the beginnings of books and the openings of letters, and it is a verse from Allah, i.e., 'I begin with Allah' every surah. And our saying 'bismi' or 'say bismi' or 'my beginning is bismi Allah', so it is good, a deleted subject. Or 'begin with Allah', its case is accusative because it is an object, and the verb that governs it is omitted because the indication of the state suffices for its mention. And it was said that the place of the ba' is nominative on the basis of a deleted subject, and its estimation is: my beginning, so the ba' here is attached to the deleted predicate that has taken its place, i.e., my beginning is established by 'bismi Allah', or it is established, then this predicate is deleted so the pronoun transfers to the place of the ba'... (Majma' al-Bayan vol.1 p.20). Or 'I seek help' or 'I intend' (Tafsir Surah al-Hamd and al-Baqara by al-Ustad al-Shahid Mutahhari p.12...]. And the derivation of 'ism' from 'sumuww', which is elevation and loftiness, and from it 'sama al-zar' meaning it grew high and rose, and from it the derivation of 'sama' (sky) due to its height and loftiness. And it is said it is derived from 'al-sima' which is the mark, so it is as if it is a mark for what it is placed for (Tafsir al-Safi vol.1 p.50, Islamic edition).
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the basmala (the phrase 'bismi Allah'). The commentator begins by explaining the orthographic peculiarity of writing 'bismi' without the alif, attributing it to frequent usage and noting that the lengthened ba' compensates for the omitted alif. He then provides historical accounts from pre-Islamic times and early Islam, citing various verses and traditions to show how the basmala was progressively revealed and standardized. He discusses the grammatical parsing of 'bismi', presenting multiple views: one considers it as an accusative object of an implied verb, another as a nominative subject with a deleted predicate. Finally, he explains the derivation of the word 'ism' (name), offering two opinions: from 'sumuww' meaning elevation, and from 'sima' meaning mark or sign. The commentary aims to justify the textual form and usage of the basmala in the opening of the book.

Key Terms
بسملة (basmala): The phrase 'bismi Allah' (in the name of Allah) used as an opening invocation. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
الاسم (al-ism): Derived either from 'sumuww' (elevation) or 'sima' (mark); it refers to the name of something. [Note: No significant shift; this is a common philological discussion.]

Key Takeaway
The basmala's orthography and grammar are deliberate, supported by historical revelation and linguistic analysis, reflecting the meticulous nature of Islamic textual tradition.

Nominal vs. Verbal in 'al-hamdu lillah' and Two Types of Precedence
Commentator discusses the choice of nominal over verbal form and types of precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two types of grammatical precedence (taqdim) and explain why the nominal form is used in 'al-hamdu lillah' based on the commentator's reasoning.

Core Idea
The nominal form conveys permanence; the precedence of 'al-hamd' is rhetorical, not essential.

The commentator explains why the author of the matn chose the nominal phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' over a verbal one. This choice is not arbitrary but carries deep rhetorical significance. Furthermore, the placement of 'al-hamd' before the name of Allah raises a subtle objection about attributing precedence to the divine, which is resolved by distinguishing two types of grammatical precedence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Forms
Locate in the Arabic where the commentator contrasts nominal (al-ismiyya) and verbal (al-fi'liyya). Note that the nominal form is said to indicate permanence (thubut) and continuity (dawam).
2
Understand the Reason for Precedence
The commentator says that although mentioning Allah is more important, the nominal form is 'stronger in terms of state' (al-ismiyya bihasb al-hal aqwā). Consider what 'state' refers to: the beginning of the book where praise is the first utterance.
The concept of 'hal' (state) relates to the context of the speech.
3
Grasp the Objection from Zamakhshari
Zamakhshari's point: 'precedence' (taqdim) and 'postponement' (ta'khir) apply only to things that can move, not to Allah. Find how the commentator resolves this by distinguishing two types of precedence.
4
Distinguish the Two Types of Taqdim
Type 1: with intention of postponement (like predicate before subject). Type 2: without intention of postponement (like subject before predicate). The precedence of 'al-hamd' is of type 2. This resolves the contradiction.
The commentator cites the author's own explanation in Sharh al-Talkhis.
5
Examine the Alternative Analysis
The commentator mentions another view: originally the sentence was verbal (ahmadu lillah hamdan), then changed to nominal, then 'al-hamd' was placed before the adverb, making it type 1 precedence. Still, no contradiction arises. Reflect on why.
This shows flexibility in grammatical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول المصنف ( الحمد الله» ... انما عدل عن الفعلية الى الاسمية، دلالة على الثبات و الدوام و اقتضاء لكلام الملك العلام، وقدم الحمد لمزيد الاهتمام به بمقتضى المقام وان كان ذكر الله اهم في نفسه فإن الاسمية بحسب الحال اقوى منها بحسب الذات و لذا لم يقدم في قوله تعالى: «فلله الحمد رب السموات و له الحمد في السموات والارض فان الغرض الاصلي هنالك بيان اختصاصه تعالى بالحمد لا اثبات الحمد له على ما اشار اليه الزمخشري فان قلت: كيف يطلق التقديم عليه وقد صرح الزمخشري بانه انما يقال مقدم ومؤخر للمزال لا للقار؟ قلت: قد اجاب المصنف عن ذلك في شرح التلخيص : بان التقديم ضربان تقديم على نية التأخير كتقديم الخبر على المبتداء وتقديم لا على نية التأخير كتقديم المبتداء على الخير وذلك بان تعمد الى اسم فتقدمه تارة على الفعل فتجعله مبتداء نحو زيد قام وتؤخره تارة فتجعله فاعلاً نحو قام زيد و تقديم الحمد من الضرب الثانى و مراد صاحب الكشاف ثمة هو الضرب الاول فلا تنافى وكلامه ايضاً مشحون باطلاق التقديم على الضرب الاول انتهى . و قد يقال ان الاصل احمد الله حمداً، فعدل من النصب الى الرفع لما ذكر، ثم قدم الحمد على الظرف فيكون ح من الضرب الأول فلا يلزم محذور اصلاً فتأمل (میرزا محمد علی)
The author's saying 'al-hamdu lillah'... he only changed from the verbal to the nominal form to indicate permanence and continuity, and to suit the speech of the King, the All-Knowing. And he prefixed 'al-hamd' (praise) for greater emphasis on it due to the context, even though mentioning Allah is more important in itself, because the nominal form in terms of state is stronger than it is in terms of essence. Therefore it was not prefixed in His statement: 'For Allah is the praise, Lord of the heavens, and to Him is praise in the heavens and the earth' because the primary purpose there is to show that the Lord is exclusive in deserving praise, not to affirm praise for Him, as al-Zamakhshari indicated. If you say: How can precedence be attributed to Him when al-Zamakhshari stated that one only says 'preceded' and 'delayed' for what is movable, not for the One who is eternal? I say: The author answered that in his commentary on al-Talkhis: precedence is of two types: precedence with the intention of postponement, like presenting the predicate before the subject, and precedence without the intention of postponement, like presenting the subject before the predicate. This is because you take a noun and sometimes present it before the verb making it a subject, as in 'Zayd stood', and sometimes postpone it making it a direct object, as in 'stood Zayd'. And the precedence of 'al-hamd' is of the second type, and what the owner of al-Kashshaf meant there is the first type, so there is no contradiction. And his words are also full of using 'precedence' for the first type, end. And it is said that the original is 'I praise Allah with praise', so he changed from accusative to nominative for what was mentioned, then he presented 'al-hamd' over the adverb, so it becomes of the first type, and no difficulty arises at all, so contemplate. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on why the author chose the nominal phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' over the verbal 'ahmadu lillah'. The commentator explains that the nominal form indicates permanence (thubut) and continuity (dawam), unlike the verbal form which implies occurrence. He then discusses the precedence of 'al-hamd' over the mention of Allah, noting that although mentioning Allah is more important, the nominal form is stronger in the present context. He addresses a potential objection from al-Zamakhshari's statement that 'precedence' cannot be attributed to Allah, and resolves it by distinguishing two types of grammatical precedence: one with intention of postponement (like predicate before subject) and one without (like subject before predicate). The precedence of 'al-hamd' is of the second type, so no contradiction. The commentary ends with an alternative analysis suggesting that the original verbal form was changed to nominal, and then 'al-hamd' was placed before the adverb, making it of the first type of precedence, but still without contradiction.

التقديم

C) Because the word 'Allah' cannot follow a verb
D) To make the sentence longer for emphasis

Key Takeaway
The nominal form in 'al-hamdu lillah' is chosen to convey permanence, and the precedence of 'al-hamd' over Allah's name is a rhetorical move justified by the absence of intention of postponement.

Two Interpretations of the Adverb in the Opening
Commentator examines whether the adverb 'after the basmala' is لغو or مستقر.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a 'laghw' and 'mustaqir' adverb and explain how each interpretation affects the meaning of key phrases in a classical text.

Core Idea
Grammar shapes theology: a single adverb choice changes the meaning of 'best speech' and resolves a hadith contradiction.

In this passage, the commentator examines the grammatical function of the adverb 'after the basmala' in the opening sentence of a book. He presents two possibilities: either it is a redundant adverb (laghw) attached to the main verb, or it is a settled adverb (mustaqir) attached to an implied clause. Each interpretation leads to different understandings of what 'best speech' (khayr al-kalam) means and how to reconcile the hadiths about beginning with praise vs. beginning with the basmala. This exercise trains you to see how careful grammatical analysis is essential for accurate interpretation in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the adverb and its context
Locate the adverb 'after the basmala' in the phrase 'he opened his book after the basmala with praise of Allah'. Understand its syntactic position.
The adverb is a zarf indicating sequence.
2
Recognize the two grammatical possibilities
The adverb can be laghw (redundant, attached to the verb 'ifta-hta') or mustaqir (settled, attached to an implied predicate 'mutalabbisan' meaning 'clothed').
Laghw is not essential; mustaqir is essential to the sentence structure.
3
Analyze implications for 'khayr al-kalam'
On the laghw interpretation, 'khayr al-kalam' means the best speech other than the basmala. On the mustaqir interpretation, it means the best speech absolutely, including the basmala.
This changes the scope of what is considered 'best speech'.
4
Apply the same to the hadith
On the laghw view, the hadith 'not begun with praise' means devoid of praise; on the mustaqir view, it means begun with Allah (the basmala suffices).
This resolves the apparent contradiction between hadiths about beginning with praise vs. beginning with basmala.
5
See how the commentator resolves the contradiction
The delay of praise after basmala does not negate opening with praise; the two hadiths address different aspects of beginning an action.
The analysis shows grammatical interpretation can harmonize textual sources.

الظرف اما لغو متعلق بافتتح يعنى افتتح كتابه بعد التيمن بالبسملة بحمد الله، أو مستقر متعلق بمتلباً محذوفاً وصلة افتتح محذوف تقديره: افتتح كتابه بالبسملة متلبساً بحمد الله بعد ها فيكون البسملة المذكورة من قبيل وضع الظاهر موضع المضمر و على الأول يكون المراد من خير الكلام خير ما عدا البسملة و على الثانى خيره مطلقاً و ايضاً على الأول يكون المراد من الحديث كل امرذى بال لم يبدء بحمد الله فهو اقطع و على الثاني كل امر ذى بال لم يبدء الله فهو ابتر فافهم و على الاول فنسبة الافتتاح بالحمد مع تأخره عن البسملة اشتغال بافادة الخفى واعراض عن ذكر الجلى او اشارة الى ان تأخر عن البسملة لا ينافي وقوع الافتتاح به فلا تعارض بين حديثى الابتداء بالتحميد والابتداء بالتسمية حقيقة فيكون هذا اجمال ما سيفضله بقوله: «فان قلت حديث الابتداء ... فيكون ذلك تفصيلاً بعد الابهام. فيكون الذ واوقع في النفوس (ميرزا محمد علی)
The adverb is either 'laghw' (redundant) attached to 'ifta-hta' meaning: he opened his book after seeking blessing with the basmala, with praise of Allah; or 'mustaqir' (settled) attached to a deleted 'mutalabbisan' (clothed), and the link of 'ifta-hta' is deleted, its estimation being: he opened his book with the basmala, clothed with praise of Allah after it. So the mentioned basmala is of the type of placing the explicit in the place of the implicit. On the first interpretation, the meaning of 'khayr al-kalam' (best speech) is the best other than the basmala, and on the second, it is the best absolutely. Also on the first, the meaning of the hadith 'every matter of importance that is not begun with praise of Allah is severed' is that, and on the second, 'every matter of importance that is not begun with Allah is truncated.' So understand. And on the first, the relation of opening with praise despite its delay after the basmala is an engagement in conveying the subtle and a turning away from mentioning the obvious, or an indication that its delay after the basmala does not contradict the occurrence of opening with it, so there is no real contradiction between the hadith of beginning with tahmid and the hadith of beginning with tasmiya. So this is the summary of what he will detail with his saying: 'then you say the hadith of beginning...' so that will be a detailed explanation after the ambiguity, and thus it is more delightful and more impactful in the souls. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and interpretive commentary on the adverb (zarf) in the opening phrase. The commentator presents two possibilities: the adverb is 'laghw' (redundant, meaning it is not essential to the sentence) and attached to the verb 'ifta-hta' (opened), meaning the author opened his book by praising Allah after seeking blessing with the basmala. Alternatively, it is 'mustaqir' (settled, meaning it provides essential information) and attached to an implied 'mutalabbisan' (clothed), meaning he opened with the basmala while being clothed with praise. Each interpretation yields different implications: on the first, 'khayr al-kalam' (best speech) refers to everything except the basmala; on the second, it includes the basmala. Similarly, the hadith about beginning with praise is interpreted accordingly. The commentator resolves an apparent contradiction between hadiths about beginning with praise vs. beginning with the basmala, explaining that the delay of praise after basmala does not negate that the book opens with praise. The passage ends by noting that this is a summary that will be detailed later.

لغو
مستقر

Introduction and Front Matter
